Chapter: Prayer of the Traveler. From the chapter of adding something to its condition, or to its place, or the action to its doer. Travel (safar) lexically is cutting distance, and in sacred law it is a specific distance with a particular journey. He indicated it by saying: (the least) duration (of travel by which) i.e., travel (the rulings) change: which are the obligation of shortening the prayer as a license of removal. Know that the license [is of two types...] etc. Lexically, it is called 'bab salat al-musafir' because it is derived from 'safar' meaning to uncover, as it uncovers the character of men. It is said: safara al-rajul yasfiru safaran, so he is safir meaning musafir. The plural is safar like raakib and rakab, sahib and sahb; it is used for the verbal noun and the plural but usage of the verb and its active participle is abandoned. Al-Safar with two fatha is the noun, its plural asfar. It means to uncover the characters of men. The mutual form is not literal because it should be between two but here it is from one. Al-Raghib said: it is literal considering that it unfolds from a place. His statement: (to its condition) in it the condition is travel, not the traveler, as cited from al-Hamawi. His statement: (or to its place) every doer has a place. His statement: (and travel lexically is cutting distance) the expression 'distance' indicates extension, so it is like what al-Sa'd said in al-Talwih: lexically it is a prolonged departure, and legally it is departure from the built-up area of one's homeland with a specific distance. End. His statement: the least duration of travel by which rulings change. Travel is of three types: travel of obedience like hajj and jihad, permissible travel like commerce, and travel of disobedience like highway robbery. The first two are causes for license by agreement. As for the latter, it is so in our school, and al-Awza'i, al-Thawri, Dawood, al-Muzani, and some Malikis agree with it, contrary to Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad who said that travel of disobedience does not grant license because it is established as a concession, and what is so does not attach to that which necessitates severity, i.e., disobedience. Mentioned by Allamah Nuh and in al-Halabi al-Kabir. For the traveler, there are rulings that differ from the resident like permission to break fast in Ramadan, extension of the wiping period to three days, and omission of Jumu'ah, the two Eids, and Udhiyah, and from that the shortening of four-rak'ah prayers. End. His statement: (which are the obligation of shortening the prayer) the pronoun refers to rulings, but this explanation is not good; better is what is in the Sharh where he said: they are the obligation of shortening the prayer, permission to break fast, extension of wiping period to three days, omission of obligation of Jumu'ah, the two Eids and Udhiyah, prohibition of a free woman traveling without a mahram, etc. End. His statement: (as a license of removal) it is better to say it is a license of removal, meaning it removes the ruling entirely without substitution. So the second pair (of rakat) is dropped from him such that he does not make it up after settling. So the fard for him is two rak'at; thus there is no change from difficulty to ease in his case. Hence it appears that the license of removal and the determination (azimah) are one in extension though different in comprehension. Hence it is said in al-Fath: whoever reports a dispute among the mashayikh that qasr is an azimah in our school or a license has made a mistake because whoever said it is a license...
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory ruling. It begins by explaining the chapter title 'Prayer of the Traveler' and the meaning of 'travel' (safar) linguistically and legally. Linguistically, safar means to uncover or to travel, and legally it is leaving one's civilized hometown with a specific distance (three days' journey or equivalent). The text then states that travel changes certain rulings, focusing on the obligation to shorten prayer as a 'license of removal' (rukhsat al-isqat). The author then divides travel into three types: obedience (hajj, jihad), permissible (trade), and disobedience (highway robbery). The first two grant licenses by consensus; the third also grants licenses according to the Hanafi school, contra the majority of other schools. The passage lists several rulings that change for the traveler: permission to break fast in Ramadan, extension of wiping over socks to three days, dropping of Jumu'ah, Eid, and Udhiyah, and shortening of four-rak'ah prayers. Finally, the author clarifies the nature of qasr: it is a 'license of removal' meaning the second two rak'at are completely dropped, so the fard for the traveler is two rak'at. This is effectively an azimah (determination) in terms of what is actually required, though conceptually it is a license. The dispute among scholars about whether qasr is a license or an azimah is dismissed as a mistake.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): Legally, departure from the built-up area of one's hometown with a specific distance (three days' journey for a camel on foot).
رخصة الإسقاط (rukhsat al-isqat): A license that completely removes the original obligation without any substitution (e.g., the second two rak'at are dropped for the traveler).
عزيمة (azimah): A ruling that is established originally, without concession. In this context, for the traveler the azimah is two rak'at, which is the same as the rukhsah in effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on shortening prayer (qasr) for a traveler?
A) It is a recommended license; the traveler may choose to shorten or complete four rak'at.
B) It is wajib; the traveler must shorten four-rak'ah prayers to two rak'at, and completing four is makruh tahrimi.
C) It is a rukhsah only for travel of obedience; for travel of disobedience, the traveler must pray full.
D) It is fard; the traveler must pray two rak'at, and four is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, qasr is a wajib license of removal (rukhsat al-isqat) for the traveler, applicable to all types of travel including sin, and it makes two rak'at the determined obligation (azimah) for the traveler.

Real vs. Metaphorical Licenses: The Case of Qasr
Defines two types of licenses and applies the distinction to prayer in travel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real license (rukhsah haqiqiyyah) and a metaphorical license (rukhsah majaziyyah) and explain why shortening prayer in travel is a metaphorical license with no choice.

Core Idea
A real license gives choice; a metaphorical license removes choice — qasr is obligatory, not optional.

In this lesson we examine the two-fold division of religious licenses (rukhsah) as presented by Imam Tahtawi. You will learn that some licenses, like breaking fast while traveling, allow a genuine choice between the easier ruling and the original determination. Others, like shortening prayer in travel, are called licenses only metaphorically because the easier ruling becomes the only valid option. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of travel and coercion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides licenses into real (rukhsah haqiqiyyah) and metaphorical (rukhsah majaziyyah). Identify the defining feature of each: choice vs. no choice.
Real licenses preserve the original determination (azimah) as an option; metaphorical licenses replace it entirely.
2
Examine the examples
For real licenses, note examples like breaking fast and uttering disbelief under duress. For metaphorical, note forced drinking and qasr. Focus on the key difference: the presence or absence of choice.
The source says in the first 'the servant is given a choice', in the second 'he has no choice'.
3
Apply the criterion to qasr
Why is qasr considered metaphorical? Because the traveler's fard is only two rak'at; there is no option to pray four. Completing four would be sinful, not rewarded.
The author argues that calling qasr a license is metaphorical because the determination (azimah) falls away completely.
4
Contrast with a real license
Compare with wiping over khuffs: a real license because the person can choose to wash feet instead. Recognize that qasr is the opposite.
This contrast helps cement the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على قسمين رخصة حقيقية ورخصة مجازية وتسمى رخصة ترفيه مثل الفطر، وإجراء كلمة الكفر بالإكراه، والثانية مثل الكره على شرب الخمر، وقصر الصلاة في السفر فالأولى العبد مخير بين ارتكاب الرخصة والعمل بالعزيمة فيثاب والثانية لا تخيير له لتعين الفعل فيها بالرخصة، وسقوط العزيمة فلا يتضمن إكمال الصلاة ثواباً لأنّ الثواب في فعل العيد ما عليه، ولو بالتخيير بينه وبين ما هو أيسر كلابس الخف فإنه مخير بين إبقائه والمسح، قلعه، والغسل، وأما الصلاة في السفر فليست إلا ركعتين من الرباعية فإذا صلاهما لم يبق عليه شيء فلا ثواب له في الإكمال أربعاً لمخالفته المفروض عليه عيناً، وإساءته بتأخير السلام، وظنه فرضية الزائدتين ولا ثواب له بالصبر على القتل، وعدم شربه الخمر بالإكراه، بل يأثم بصبره، وتسمية هذه وتسمية القصر في السفر رخصة مجاز لأن الرخصة الحقيقية يثبت معها الخيار للعبد بين الإقدام على الرخصة وبين الإتيان بالعزيمة كالمسح على الخف كما ذكرناه والفطر في رمضان وسقوط وجوب الجمعة، والعيدين
...into two divisions: a real license and a metaphorical license; and it is also called a license of relaxation (rukhsat tarkhif). Examples of the first: breaking fast (in Ramadan) and uttering a word of disbelief under duress. The second is like being forced to drink wine, and shortening prayer in travel. In the first, the servant is given a choice between performing the license and acting upon the determination (azimah) and is rewarded. In the second, he has no choice because the action is determined by the license and the determination falls away. So completing the prayer (to four) does not entail reward because the reward is in doing what he is commanded to do, even if with a choice between it and something easier – like the wearer of khuffs who has a choice between keeping them, wiping over them, removing them, and washing. As for prayer in travel, it is only two rak'at from the four, so when he prays them nothing remains upon him. Thus he has no reward for completing four because he would be opposing what is specifically obligated upon him, and he would be sinning by delaying the salam and thinking the extra two are obligatory. And there is no reward for enduring being killed and not drinking wine under duress; rather he sins by his endurance. Calling these and calling qasr in travel a metaphorical license is because the real license involves choice for the servant between undertaking the license and performing the determination – like wiping over khuffs as mentioned, and breaking fast in Ramadan, and the dropping of the obligation of Jumu'ah and the two Eids.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling that classifies religious licenses (rukhsah) into two categories. First, a real license (rukhsah haqiqiyyah) where the servant has a genuine choice between the easier license and the original determination, and is rewarded for either. Examples are breaking fast in Ramadan (with intention) and uttering disbelief under coercion. Second, a metaphorical license (rukhsah majaziyyah), also called a license of relaxation, where there is no choice because the license effectively replaces the determination entirely. The most important example is shortening prayer (qasr) in travel. The author explains that for the traveler, his obligation is only two rak'at; there is no alternative of four. Therefore, completing four would be sinful (makruh tahrimi) and brings no extra reward. This differs from real licenses like wiping over khuffs, where the person could choose to wash feet instead. The passage clarifies that qasr is called a license only metaphorically because it actually becomes the determined obligation (azimah) for the traveler, thus there is no choice.

Key Terms
رخصة حقيقية (rukhsah haqiqiyyah): A real license where the servant has a genuine choice between the easier license and the original determination (azimah), being rewarded for either.
رخصة مجازية (rukhsah majaziyyah): A metaphorical license where there is no choice; the license replaces the determination entirely, so it is effectively the azimah under a different name.
رخصة ترفيه (rukhsat tarkhif): Another name for the metaphorical license, emphasizing ease (tarkhif).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a real license (rukhsah haqiqiyyah) according to this passage?
A) Shortening prayer in travel.
B) Breaking the fast in Ramadan while traveling.
C) Forced drinking of wine.
D) Neglecting to wash feet when wiping over khuffs.

Key Takeaway
A real license offers choice and reward for either option; a metaphorical license like qasr removes choice and makes the easier ruling obligatory.

The Two Types of Rukhsah: Real and Metaphorical
Defines and exemplifies the two categories of rukhsah in Hanafi fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real (haqiqi) and metaphorical (majazi) rukhsah and identify the ruling of each.

Core Idea
Real rukhsah offers a choice; metaphorical rukhsah mandates the easier ruling.

This lesson explores the Hanafi classification of rukhsah. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it determines whether you have a choice to follow the easier ruling or are obliged to take it. The text from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the nature of concessions like breaking fast and shortening prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that rukhsah is divided into real (haqiqi) and metaphorical (majazi).
2
Analyze the nature of real rukhsah
In real rukhsah, the servant has a choice between taking the concession and acting with determination (azimah), and is rewarded for the latter.
Examples: breaking fast in travel, wiping over khuff.
3
Analyze the nature of metaphorical rukhsah
In metaphorical rukhsah, there is no choice; the concession is obligatory and the azimah is overridden.
Examples: drinking wine under coercion, shortening prayer in travel.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
Use the text's examples to test your understanding: e.g., why is shortening prayer metaphorical?

الإمام خصوصاً المخالف للنص الصريح عنه، وعن بعض أصحابنا تقديرها بخمسة عشر فرسخاً قوله: بسير وسط فلو أسرع بريدة فقطع ما يقطع بالسير الوسط في ثلاثة أيام في أقل منها قصر وكما إذا سار فيها سيراً سيراً خارقاً للعادة، في التبيين أنه يكتفي في تقدير المسافة بالمدة المذكورة بغلبة الظن، ولا يشترط اليقين اهـ. قوله: لأن الليل ليس محلاً للسير قال القهستاني الأولى ترك ذكر الليالي لأنها للإستراحة. قوله: ولا بذ الخ محل الإشتراط قوله مع الاستراحات والسين والتاء فيها زائدتان. قوله: وسار إلى ما بعد الزوال الذي في عبارة غيره التسوية بين الأيام الثلاثة في اعتبار الزوال واعلم أن الزوال أكثر النهار الشرعي الذي هو من الفجر إلى الغروب، وهو نصف النهار الفلكي الذي هو من الطلوع إلى الغروب، ثم إنّ من الفجر إلى الزوال في أقصر أيام السنة في مصر، وما ساواها في العرض سبع ساعات إلا ربعاً فمجموع الثلاثة أيام عشرون ساعة وربع اه ذكره صاحب تحفة الأخيار. قوله: وهو سير الأبل أي ابل القافلة بدليل قوله ومشي الأقدام . قوله: في البر متعلق بقوله اعتبر قوله: وعراً أي صعباً شاقاً. قوله: من ابتداء اليوم
The Book of Prayer. It is of two types: real concession and metaphorical concession, called a concession of ease, like breaking fast and uttering words of disbelief under coercion. The second is like being forced to drink wine and shortening prayer in travel. The first: the servant is free to choose between indulging in the concession and acting with determination, and he is rewarded. The second: he has no choice because the action is determined by the concession and the determination falls, so completing the prayer does not include reward because reward lies in doing what is obligatory upon him, even if by choice between it and what is easier, like the one wearing khuff: he is free to choose between keeping it and wiping, removing and washing. As for prayer in travel, it is only two rak'ahs of the four-rak'ah prayer; so if he prays them, nothing remains upon him, so no reward for him in completing four due to his violation of what is obligatory upon him specifically, and his bad action by delaying the salam, and his assumption that the extra two are obligatory. And no reward for him in patience upon killing, and not drinking wine under coercion; rather he sins by his patience. And naming these and naming shortening in travel as metaphorical concession because the real concession establishes choice for the servant between proceeding with the concession and acting with determination, like wiping over khuff as we mentioned, and breaking fast in Ramadan, and the falling of obligation of Friday and the two Eids and sacrifice, and no choice for him between drinking wine under coercion and patience upon his killing, nor between completing the four-rak'ah prayer and shortening it in travel of a distance of three days from the shortest days of the year, and it is measured by days without stages and parasangs, and this is the most correct, by moderate walking, because the night is not a place for travel but for rest. It is necessary that the travel be during the day with rest stops; the traveler descends for eating, drinking, answering nature's call, and prayer. And the major part of the day has the ruling of all of it. So if he sets out intending a place, and sets out early on the first day and travels until midday until he reaches the stage, then dismounts for rest and spends the night there, then sets out early on the second day and travels until after midday and dismounts, then sets out early on the third and travels until midday and reaches the destination. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: the correct is that he is a traveler. And the moderate walking is considered, which is the walking of camels and walking of feet on land. And in the mountain it is considered accordingly because it includes ascents, descents, narrow passages, and rugged terrain, so the walking of camels and feet there is less than their walking on level ground. So if he traverses by that walking a distance not far from the beginning of the day and dismounts after midday, it is counted as a day according to what we have presented. Then when he spends the night, then by genitive linking to 'wiping', indeed the traveler if he prays Friday, the two Eids, and offers sacrifice, it is valid and he is rewarded. His statement: 'And no choice for him, etc.' rather he is obliged to drink and shorten. His statement: 'three days journey' this is the estimation for travel in which prayer is shortened, fasting is permitted, wiping more than a day and night is permitted, and sacrifice falls. But what permits leaving Friday, the two Eids, and congregation, and permits performing supererogatory prayers on an animal and tayammum, and istihbab of lottery between his wives, is not estimated by this duration. His statement: 'without stages and parasangs' it is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that it is estimated by three stages. Al-Hidayah said: it is close to the first because the custom is that each day's travel is a stage. His statement: 'and this is the most correct' he said in al-Bahr: I am amazed at their fatwas in this and similar matters that oppose the Imam's madhhab, especially contradictory to the explicit text from him. And from some of our companions, its estimation is fifteen parasangs. His statement: 'by moderate walking' if he sped quickly and covered what is covered by moderate walking in three days in less than that, he shortens. And also if he walked in it a walking that is unusual breaking the norm. In al-Tabyin: he is content in estimating the distance by the mentioned duration based on predominant assumption, and certainty is not required. End. His statement: 'because the night is not a place for travel' al-Qahistani said: the first is to leave mentioning the nights because they are for rest. His statement: 'and there is no...' the place of condition. His statement: 'with the rest stops' and the sin and ta' in it are extra. His statement: 'and he traveled until after midday' in the expression of others is equating the three days in considering midday. Know that midday is the majority of the legal day which is from dawn to sunset, and it is half of the astronomical day which is from sunrise to sunset. Then from dawn to midday in the shortest days of the year in Egypt and what is equal to it in latitude is seven hours minus a quarter, so the total of the three days is twenty hours and a quarter. End. Mentioned by the author of Tuhfat al-Akhyar. His statement: 'and it is the walking of camels' i.e., the camels of the caravan, as evidence of his saying 'and walking of feet'. His statement: 'on the land' is related to his saying 'considered'. His statement: 'rugged' i.e., difficult, arduous. His statement: 'from the beginning of the day'
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the classification of rukhsah (concession) into two types: haqiqi (real) and majazi (metaphorical). The real concession offers a choice between the easier ruling and the original determination, while the metaphorical concession mandates the easier ruling without choice. Examples include breaking fast in travel (real) and shortening prayer (metaphorical). The author then details the conditions for qasr, specifying the travel distance as three days' journey by moderate walking, clarifying that nights are for rest and that the calculation is based on the shortest days of the year. The commentary emphasizes that for qasr, there is no choice to complete four rak'ahs, and the traveler must shorten. The text also addresses disagreements regarding measurement by stages or parasangs, noting that the relied-upon view is by days with moderate walking.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsah): A concession that allows an easier ruling due to an excuse, categorized into real (with choice) and metaphorical (without choice).
عزيمة (azimah): The original ruling without excuse; acting with determination despite a concession is rewarded.
قصر الصلاة (qasr al-salah): Shortening the four-rak'ah prayer to two rak'ahs during travel of at least three days' journey.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a metaphorical rukhsah according to the text?
A) Breaking fast in Ramadan while traveling
B) Wiping over khuff during wudu
C) Shortening prayer in travel
D) Choosing to fast while traveling

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school divides rukhsah into real (with choice) and metaphorical (without choice); knowing which type applies determines the legal obligation of the individual.

Salah — Prayer

Conditions for Qasr Prayer: The Minimum Travel Distance
Details the measurement of travel distance for the obligatory shortening of prayer (qasr).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum travel distance for qasr according to the relied-upon Hanafi view and explain the method of measurement.

Core Idea
Qasr becomes wajib only for a journey of three days by moderate walking, measured by days of travel with rest stops.

This lesson delves into the practical conditions that trigger the obligation to shorten prayers. The text from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the measurement of travel distance, the controversy over stages versus days, and the emphasis on moderate walking. Understanding these details ensures a traveler knows exactly when qasr is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minimum travel distance
The text states that the travel must be three days' journey from the shortest days of the year, measured by moderate walking.
Three days by moderate walking (sail al-wasat) is the relied-upon view.
2
Understand the measurement by days, not stages
The distance is not measured by stages (marahil) or parasangs (farasikh), but by days of travel.
Text explicitly says 'wa huwa al-asahh' (the most correct).
3
Account for rest stops and nights
Nights are for rest, not travel. The day's travel includes rest stops for eating, prayer, etc.
The traveler must travel each day from dawn until midday with breaks.
4
Consider moderate walking and terrain
The standard is the walking speed of camels and men on level ground; mountains and rugged terrain require adjustment.
Al-Sarakhsi confirms this.
5
Apply the intention and timing
The traveler must intend to travel this distance from the start and must actually cover it with the prescribed daily routine.
If he covers the distance faster, it still counts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة
على قسمين رخصة حقيقية ورخصة مجازية وتسمى رخصة ترفيه مثل الفطر، وإجراء كلمة الكفر بالإكراه، والثانية مثل الكره على شرب الخمر، وقصر الصلاة في السفر فالأولى العبد مخير بين ارتكاب الرخصة والعمل بالعزيمة فيثاب والثانية لا تخيير له لتعين الفعل فيها بالرخصة، وسقوط العزيمة فلا يتضمن إكمال الصلاة ثواباً لأنّ الثواب في فعل العيد ما عليه، ولو بالتخيير بينه وبين ما هو أيسر كلابس الخف فإنه مخير بين إبقائه والمسح، قلعه، والغسل، وأما الصلاة في السفر فليست إلا ركعتين من الرباعية فإذا صلاهما لم يبق عليه شيء فلا ثواب له في الإكمال أربعاً لمخالفته المفروض عليه عيناً، وإساءته بتأخير السلام، وظنه فرضية الزائدتين ولا ثواب له بالصبر على القتل، وعدم شربه الخمر بالإكراه، بل يأثم بصبره، وتسمية هذه وتسمية القصر في السفر رخصة مجاز لأن الرخصة الحقيقية يثبت معها الخيار للعبد بين الإقدام على الرخصة وبين الإتيان بالعزيمة كالمسح على الخف كما ذكرناه والفطر في رمضان وسقوط وجوب الجمعة، والعيدين والأضحية، ولا تخيير له بين شرب الخمر مكرهاً وصبره على قتله ولا بين إكمال الصلاة الرباعية وقصره بالسفر مسيرة ثلاثة أيام من أقصر أيام السنة وقدر بالأيام دون المراحل، والفراسخ وهو الأصح بسير وسط سير لأنّ الليل ليس محلاً للسير، بل للإستراحة ولا بد أن يكون السير نهاراً مع الاستراحات فينزل المسافر فيه للأكل والشرب، وقضاء الضرورة والصلاة، ولأكثر النهار حكم كله فإذا خرج قاصداً محلاً، وبكر في اليوم الأول وسار إلى وقت الزوال حتى بلغ المرحلة، فنزل بها للإستراحة وبات بها ثم يكر في اليوم الثاني وسار إلى ما بعد الزوال ونزل ثم بكر في الثالث وسار إلى الزوال فبلغ المقصد قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي الصحيح أنه مسافر و اعتبر السير الوسط وهو سير الإبل ومشي الأقدام في البر و يعتبر في الجبل بما يناسبه لأنه يكون صعوداً وهبوطاً ومضيقاً ووعراً فيكون مشي الإبل والأقدام فيه دون سيرهما في السهل فإذا قطع بذلك السير مسافة ليست ببعيدة من ابتداء اليوم، ونزل بعد الزوال احتسب به على نحو ما قدمناه يوماً فإذا بات، ثم بالجر عطفاً على المسح ، فإنّ المسافر إذا صلى الجمعة، والعيدين وضحى صح منه ذلك وأثيب. قوله: ولا تخيير له الخ بل يتعين عليه الشرب، والقصر. قوله: مسيرة ثلاثة أيام هذا التقدير للسفر الذي تقصر فيه الصلاة، ويباح فيه الفطر، ويمسح فيه أكثر من يوم وليلة، وتسقط به الأضحية وأما المبيح لترك الجمعة والعيدين والجماعة، والمبيح للتنفل على الدابة وللتيمم، ولاستحباب القرعة بين نسائه فلا يقدر بهذه المدة. قوله: دون المراحل، والفراسخ روي عن الإمام أنها مقدرة بثلاثة مراحل قال في الهداية : وهو قريب من الأول لأنّ المعتاد في السير كل يوم مرحلة قوله: وهو الأصح قال في البحر وأنا أتعجب من فتواهم في هذا، وأمثاله بما يخالف مذهب الإمام خصوصاً المخالف للنص الصريح عنه، وعن بعض أصحابنا تقديرها بخمسة عشر فرسخاً قوله: بسير وسط فلو أسرع بريدة فقطع ما يقطع بالسير الوسط في ثلاثة أيام في أقل منها قصر وكما إذا سار فيها سيراً سيراً خارقاً للعادة، في التبيين أنه يكتفي في تقدير المسافة بالمدة المذكورة بغلبة الظن، ولا يشترط اليقين اهـ. قوله: لأن الليل ليس محلاً للسير قال القهستاني الأولى ترك ذكر الليالي لأنها للإستراحة. قوله: ولا وربع اه ذكره صاحب تحفة الأخيار. قوله: وهو سير الأبل أي ابل القافلة بدليل قوله ومشي الأقدام . قوله: في البر متعلق بقوله اعتبر قوله: وعراً أي صعباً شاقاً. قوله: من ابتداء اليوم
The Book of Prayer. It is of two types: real concession and metaphorical concession, called a concession of ease, like breaking fast and uttering words of disbelief under coercion. The second is like being forced to drink wine and shortening prayer in travel. The first: the servant is free to choose between indulging in the concession and acting with determination, and he is rewarded. It is necessary that the travel be during the day with rest stops; the traveler descends for eating, drinking, answering nature's call, and prayer. This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the classification of rukhsah (concession) into two types: haqiqi (real) and majazi (metaphorical). Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
Travel Distance for Qasr: Three Days Moderate Pace
Defines travel distance for shortening prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi definition of travel distance for qasr and explain why the three-day standard is preferred over alternative Hanafi narrations.

Core Idea
Travel for qasr is three days at moderate pace; nights do not count as travel.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya settles an intra-Hanafi dispute over the minimum distance that constitutes travel for shortening prayers. The author clarifies that the relied-upon view is a three-day journey with moderate pace, rejecting alternative measures such as three stages or fifteen farsakh. Understanding this standard is essential for determining when a traveler may perform qasr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses the question: what distance triggers the permissibility (and obligation) of qasr? This is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
2
State the mufta bihi position
Al-Tahtawi explicitly states the soundest view is three days of travel with a moderate pace (sair awsat), measured during daylight hours only.
Nights are for rest, not travel.
3
Describe the moderate pace
The pace is defined as the walking speed of camels and people on foot, not the fastest (barid) nor slowest (cart). For mountains, adjust appropriately.
Standard is 'best of matters' — camels and feet.
4
Account for the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some narrations from Abu Hanifa mention three stages (marahil) or fifteen farsakh. The passage prefers the three-day measurement as more widely transmitted and practical.
Tarjih authority: al-Ashah (the soundest).
5
Apply the ruling in practice
If a traveler covers most of the day at moderate pace, it counts as a full day even if actual distance is less. This standard is used for sea travel with moderate wind.
Thamarah al-khilaf: different distances affect permissibility of qasr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the sacrifice, and he has no choice between drinking wine under duress and enduring his killing, nor between completing the four-rak'ah prayer and shortening it during travel (a journey of three days from the shortest days of the year, and it is measured by days not by stages or farsakh, and the soundest view is with moderate pace) during the day because the night is not a place for travel, but for rest, and travel must be during the day with rests, so the traveler dismounts for eating, drinking, relieving nature, and prayer, and most of the day has the ruling of the whole. So if he sets out intending a place, and starts early on the first day and travels until the time of zawal until he reaches the stage, then dismounts for rest and stays overnight, then starts early on the second day and travels until after zawal and dismounts, then starts early on the third and travels until zawal and reaches the destination. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: the correct view is that he is a traveler. And the moderate pace is considered, which is the pace of camels and walking on foot on land, and in mountains what is appropriate because it involves ascent and descent, narrow and rugged terrain, so the walking of camels and feet there is less than their pace on the plain. So if by that pace he covers a distance not far from the beginning of the day, and dismounts after zawal, it is counted as a day according to what we have presented. Then if he stays overnight, then becomes in the morning and does likewise until after zawal, then dismounts, that is a second day. The fastest pace, which is the barid (courier), is not considered, nor the slowest pace, which is the walking of a cart pulled by animals, for the best of matters is camels and feet, as we mentioned. And in Al-Bahr, moderate wind is considered, according to its median pace, here it is the pace that is the mufta bihi. So if he travels most of the day at that pace, it is as if he traveled the whole day, even if the distance is less than on the plain.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya discusses the minimum distance that constitutes travel (safar) for the purpose of shortening prayers (qasr). The author, Imam al-Tahtawi, clarifies that the standard is three days of travel with a moderate pace, not counting nights as travel time. The ruling is based on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his Companions. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some narrations from Abu Hanifa estimate three stages (marahil), while others say fifteen farsakh. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the three-day measurement. The commentary emphasizes that the pace must be moderate—neither too fast like a courier (barid) nor too slow like a cart. For sea travel, the wind condition is considered. This lesson establishes the foundational rule for when a traveler may shorten prayers.

Key Terms
سير وسط (sair awsat): Moderate pace, neither fast nor slow; specifically the pace of camels and walking feet.
مرحلة (marhalah): A stage of travel, approximately one day's journey.
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance; fifteen farsakh was another estimated measure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, what is the minimum travel distance for qasr?
A) Three stages (marahil)
B) Fifteen farsakh
C) Three days with moderate pace
D) One day and night

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for travel distance is three days at moderate pace, measured during daylight, rejecting alternative stage and farsakh measures.

Salah — Prayer

Qasr al-Salah: Which Prayers Are Shortened and Conditions for the Traveler
Rulings on which prayers are shortened and conditions for qasr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which prayers a traveler shortens and explain the Hanafi proof and conditions for qasr.

Core Idea
Only four-rak'ah fard prayers are shortened; the traveler's intention to travel suffices even if the journey is sinful.

Qasr (shortening prayer) is a divine concession for travelers, but it is precisely bounded. This lesson examines exactly which prayers are shortened, when the traveler should pray sunan, and the key condition of travel intention. Understanding these boundaries ensures that the rukhsah is applied correctly without excess or neglect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is qasr of four-rak'ah fard prayers. This is wajib for the traveler who meets the conditions.
The term 'fard 'ilmi' refers to prayers established by definitive evidence; these are Zuhr, Asr, and Isha.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Aisha (RA): 'Prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs each, then it was increased in residence and left as is in travel.'
This hadith is found in Sahih Muslim and Sahih al-Bukhari.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith indicates that the original prescription was two rak'ahs, and the increase to four was only for residents. Therefore, travelers are not required to perform the increased rak'ahs; they must pray two.
The imperative for travelers is to pray two, making qasr wajib.
4
Note the exceptions
Fajr (2 rak'ahs), Maghrib (3 rak'ahs), and Witr are not shortened. Jumu'ah also remains at 2 because of the sermon. Sunan are omitted if the traveler is actively journeying or fearful.
Maghrib is called 'witr of the day' because it is odd-numbered and performed after daytime prayers.
5
Note the condition for qasr
The condition is simply the intention of travel (nawā al-safar). Even if the travel is sinful (e.g., a runaway slave), qasr is still allowed.
This is a unique Hanafi position; other schools may require a minimum distance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فيقصر) المسافر (الفرض) العلمي (الرباعي) فلا قصر للثنائي، والثلاثي ولا للوتر فإنه فرض عملي، ولا في السنن فإن كان في حال نزول وقرار وأمن يأتي بالسنن وإن كان سائراً أو خائفاً فلا يأتي بها وهو المختار قالت عائشة رضي الله عنها فرضت الصلاة ركعتين ركعتين فزيدت في الحضر، وأقرت في السفر إلا المغرب فإنها وتر النهار ، والجمعة لمكانها من الخطبة، والصبح لطول قراءتها وعندنا يقصر من نوى السفر ولو كان عاصياً بسفره كابق من سيده
The traveler shortens the four-rak'ah obligatory prayer (the scientific fard). So there is no shortening for the two-rak'ah or three-rak'ah prayers, nor for witr, for it is a practical fard. Nor for the sunan. If he is in a state of halting, settled, and secure, he performs the sunan; if he is traveling or fearful, he does not perform them, and that is the chosen view. Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said: 'Prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs each, then it was increased in residence and left as is in travel, except for Maghrib for it is the witr of the day, and Jumu'ah because of its sermon, and Fajr because of its lengthy recitation.' According to us, one who intends travel shortens even if he is disobedient in his travel, like a runaway slave.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya specifies which prayers a traveler shortens. The ruling is that only four-rak'ah fard prayers are shortened to two. Two-rak'ah prayers (Fajr), three-rak'ah (Maghrib), and witr are not shortened. The hadeeth of Aisha (RA) is cited as proof: prayers were originally two rak'ahs, then increased for residents but kept at two for travelers, except Maghrib (as it is the witr of the day) and Fajr (due to its long recitation). Sunan prayers are optional: if the traveler is settled and secure, he prays them; if traveling or fearful, he omits them. Additionally, the condition for qasr is the intention of travel, even if the journey is sinful (e.g., a runaway slave). This is the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
فرض علمي (fard 'ilmi): A fard that is established by definitive evidence (qat'i); here refers to the four-rak'ah prayers.
وتر النهار (witr al-nahar): Maghrib is called the witr of the day because it is odd-numbered (three rak'ahs) and performed after the daytime prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following prayers does a traveler shorten according to the Hanafi school?
A) Fajr
B) Maghrib
C) Zuhr
D) Witr

Key Takeaway
Only four-rak'ah fard prayers are shortened, and the traveler's intention to travel suffices even if the travel is sinful.

Pace of Travel and Which Prayers Are Shortened
Explaining the average pace considered for travel distance and the ruling on shortening prayers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the pace of travel for qasr and identify which prayers are shortened and under what conditions.

Core Idea
Shortening the four-rak'ah fard prayers is wajib for a traveler; sunnah are omitted when traveling or fearful.

The Hanafi school defines the travel distance based on an average pace. This lesson examines the pace considered for qasr and the specific prayers that are shortened, supported by a hadith of Aisha (ra).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is qasr (shortening) during travel. The Hanafi ruling is that shortening the four-rak'ah fard prayers (Zuhr, Asr, Isha) is wajib. Performing them fully is makruh tahrimi.
Shortening applies only to four-rak'ah obligatory prayers, not to Fajr, Maghrib, or witr.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Aisha (ra) that prayer was originally two rak'ahs and increased only for residents. This shows that shortening is the original rule for travelers.
The hadith is ahad, making it zanni al-thubut, hence the ruling is wajib, not fard.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'و أقرت في السفر' (and it was confirmed in travel) indicates that the two-rak'ah form was maintained for travel. The imperative to pray two rak'ahs implies obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul.
The exception for Maghrib and Fajr confirms that only four-rak'ah prayers are shortened.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that qasr is optional (rukhasah jawaaz), not obligatory. They interpret the hadith as permissibility, not obligation.
The Hanafi position relies on the principle that a command (amr) indicates obligation unless there is evidence otherwise.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Al-Tahtawi cites al-Bahr and declares the average pace as the mufta bihi standard. The hadith, combined with Hanafi usul, establishes wujub. The chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that sunnah prayers are omitted when traveling or fearful.
The pace of travel is defined as the average pace (sawt al-awsat), and if most of the day is traveled at this pace, it counts as a full day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما ذكرناه ( وفي البحر) يعتبر (اعتدال الريح) على سير أوساطها، هنا وهو سير المفتي به فإذا سار أكثر اليوم به كان ككله، وإن كانت المسافة دون ما في السهل (فيقصر) المسافر (الفرض) العلمي (الرباعي) فلا قصر للثنائي، والثلاثي ولا للوتر فإنه فرض عملي، ولا في السنن فإن كان في حال نزول وقرار وأمن يأتي بالسنن وإن كان سائراً أو خائفاً فلا يأتي بها وهو المختار قالت عائشة رضي الله عنها فرضت الصلاة ركعتين ركعتين فزيدت في الحضر، وأقرت في السفر إلا المغرب فإنها وتر النهار، والجمعة لمكانها من الخطبة، والصبح لطول قراءتها وعندنا يقصر من نوى السفر ولو كان عاصياً بسفره) كابق من سيده
As we mentioned (And in al-Bahr) it is considered (moderation of wind) on the average pace of travel, here it is the pace on which fatwa is given. So if he traveled most of the day with it, it is as if the whole day. And if the distance is less than that on land, (then the traveler shortens) the (obligatory) (four-rak'ah) (scientific) prayer. So there is no shortening for the two-rak'ah or three-rak'ah, nor for witr because it is an obligatory act, nor in the sunnah prayers. So if he is in a state of descending and staying and safe, he performs the sunnah prayers, but if he is traveling or fearful, he does not perform them. That is the chosen position. Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, said: 'Prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs, two rak'ahs, then it was increased in residence and confirmed in travel, except for Maghrib because it is the witr of the day, and Jumu'ah because of its sermon, and Fajr because of its lengthy recitation.' And according to us, one shortens if he intends travel, even if he is disobedient in his travel, like a runaway from his master.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi discusses the pace of travel for determining the distance of safr. The author states that the relied-upon (mufta bihi) pace is the average pace (sawt al-awsat). If a traveler covers most of the day at this pace, it is considered as if he traveled the whole day. Then it transitions to the ruling on which prayers are shortened: only the four-rak'ah obligatory prayers (Zuhr, Asr, Isha) are shortened; two-rak'ah (Fajr), three-rak'ah (Maghrib), and witr are not shortened because witr is an obligatory act (fard 'amali) but not a four-rak'ah prayer. Sunnah prayers are only performed if the traveler is settled and safe; if traveling or fearful, they are omitted. The proof is the hadith of Aisha (ra) that prayers were originally two rak'ahs and increased only for residents. The passage also confirms that even a disobedient traveler (e.g., a runaway slave) may shorten if he intends travel.

Key Terms
سير أوساطها (sayr awsatiha): The average pace of travel, neither very fast nor very slow.
فرض علمي (fard 'ilmi): An obligation established by definitive evidence (qat'i) — here referring to the four-rak'ah prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that shortening the four-rak'ah fard prayers is wajib for a traveler, based on the hadith of Aisha, and sunnah prayers are omitted when traveling or fearful.

Intention for Travel and the Disobedient Traveler
Clarifying the requirement of intention and the ruling for disobedient travelers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition of intention for qasr and apply the ruling to both obedient and disobedient travelers, including the relied-upon position when intention arises during prayer.

Core Idea
Firm intention to travel before prayer is required; even sinful travel allows shortening.

This lesson from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies a key condition for the concession of shortening prayer (qasr): the traveler must have a firm intention to travel. The author emphasizes that intention must be present before the prayer begins and that even a disobedient traveler (e.g., a runaway slave) qualifies. An important intra-Hanafi difference is resolved regarding what happens if one intends travel during a prayer already begun in residence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Qasr (shortening the four-rak'ah fard to two rak'ahs) is wajib when the conditions of travel are met. Confirm that the act under discussion is the prayer of the traveler.
The Hanafi school considers qasr obligatory, not merely permissible.
2
State the condition of intention
A firm intention (niyya) to travel a distance of three days' journey is required. The intention must be made before the prayer begins. Without intention, one is not a traveler even if covering great distances.
Intention must be connected to the act of travel; mere wandering does not count.
3
Note the exception for disobedient travel
Even if the travel itself is sinful (e.g., running away from one's master), the traveler still shortens. The concession is general.
This is based on the generality of the Quranic verse (4:101).
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on timing
If a person begins prayer while in residence (e.g., on a docked ship) and during the prayer the wind carries the ship away, causing him to intend travel—Abu Yusuf says complete the prayer as a resident (four rak'ahs) because the cause for completion was present at the start. Muhammad says shorten. The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's.
The dispute is rational (dalil aqli) based on conflicting causes: cause for completion vs. cause for shortening.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa: intention must be firm and before prayer; if intention arises during prayer, complete as resident (Abu Yusuf). Also, intention of a minor is invalid until puberty; intention of a disbeliever upon conversion is valid.
Al-Tahtawi cites al-Qahistani for this tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage from the hashya explains the condition of intention (niyya) for becoming a traveler. The author emphasizes that a firm intention to travel is necessary; intention must be present before the prayer begins. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if one begins prayer while still in residence (e.g., on a ship at the dock) and the wind carries the ship away, causing him to intend travel during the prayer, Abu Yusuf says he completes as a resident (full prayer) because the cause for completion (residence at start) is present, while Muhammad says he should shorten. The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's out of precaution. The intention of a minor (sabi) is not valid for travel until puberty, but a disbeliever's intention to travel is valid upon conversion. The passage also clarifies that the intention must be connected to the act of travel; mere wandering without intention does not make one a traveler. It also discusses the ruling for a disobedient traveler: even if traveling for sin (e.g., running away from master), he may shorten, based on the general application of the Quranic concession for travel.

Key Terms
نية السفر (niyya al-safar): A firm intention to travel a distance that permits qasr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a person begins prayer while still in residence, then the ship starts moving and he intends travel during the prayer, what should he do?
A) Complete the prayer as a resident (four rak'ahs)
B) Shorten to two rak'ahs immediately
C) Break the prayer and restart
D) Continue as he is, no change required

Key Takeaway
Firm intention before prayer is essential for qasr; even sinful travel qualifies, and if intention arises during prayer, complete as resident (Abu Yusuf).

Boundary Conditions for Becoming a Traveler
Explaining which buildings must be passed to be considered a traveler

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three boundary conditions (houses, fina', rabad) that must be passed for a person to be considered a traveler in the Hanafi school, and distinguish the exceptional case where the fina' is separated by a bowshot.

Core Idea
A traveler's prayer is shortened only after passing the built-up area including suburb and attached courtyard.

This lesson examines the precise physical boundaries that define when a resident becomes a traveler for the purpose of shortening prayers (qasr). The Hanafi school has detailed conditions involving the houses of the town, the attached courtyard (fina'), and the suburb (rabad). We also encounter an internal dispute regarding the status of the courtyard, with some authorities holding that leaving the inhabited area suffices without needing to pass the fina' separately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act under discussion is leaving one's permanent residence to become a traveler, which triggers the allowance to shorten prayers. The ruling is that qasr becomes permissible once specific conditions are met.
The ruling is a rukhsah (concession), not an obligation.
2
List the conditions according to the main view
The passage states three conditions: passing the houses of the town (even if tents), passing the attached courtyard (fina'), and passing the suburb (rabad) including connected villages.
Being parallel to houses on one side only suffices to be considered passed.
3
Note the exception for a separated courtyard
If the courtyard is separated by a farm or empty space of a bowshot length (300-400 steps), passing the courtyard is not necessary. The houses alone suffice.
This is the relied-upon view according to Qadi Khan and others.
4
Recognize the internal ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi authorities (al-Nihaya, al-Fatawa al-Walwaljiyya, etc.) hold that leaving the inhabited area ('umran) is sufficient, and the courtyard is not part of the city for travel rulings. They distinguish between Jumu'ah (where courtyard is included) and travel (where it is not).
This is a legitimate difference within the school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the view requiring passing the houses, courtyard, and suburb is the correct one (al-sahih). The preponderant position is to require all three unless the courtyard is separated by a bowshot.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

And it is also necessary that he has passed what is attached to his residence from his courtyard, just as it is necessary to pass its suburb (rabad) which is the houses and dwellings around the city, for it is considered part of the city. Likewise, villages connected to the suburb of the city must be passed in the correct view. And if the courtyard is separated by a farm or an open space of a bowshot length – and it was stated that it is from three hundred to four hundred steps – then passing the courtyard is not necessary. Also, if a village is connected to the courtyard but not to the suburb, passing it is not necessary; rather, passing the courtyard suffices. This is in Qadi Khan, but it is contradicted by what is in al-Nihaya, al-Fatawa al-Walwaljiyya, al-Tajniss, and al-Mazid, which state: he shortens by leaving the inhabited area of the city, and the courtyard of the city is not considered part of the city regarding travel, but it is considered part of the city for the validity of Jumu'ah prayer. Commentary
This passage from the hashya details the conditions of exiting the city (misd) to become a traveler. The principle is that one must pass the houses of the town, including tents (tents are considered houses for Bedouins). Being parallel to the buildings on one side only does not prevent one from being considered a traveler if he has left the built-up area. Additionally, the courtyard (fina') attached to one's residence is considered part of the city, so one must pass it. Similarly, the suburb (rabad) – the surrounding dwellings – is also part of the city. Villages that are connected to the suburb must also be passed. However, if the courtyard is separated by a farm or open space of a bowshot length (approx 300-400 steps), one does not need to pass the courtyard separately (i.e., once you have left the houses, you are considered out). There is a difference of opinion: according to Qadi Khan, if a village is connected to the courtyard but not the suburb, passing the village is not required; passing the courtyard suffices. But other sources (al-Nihaya, etc.) hold that leaving the inhabited area ('umran) is sufficient, and the courtyard is not part of the city for travel (but is for Jumu'ah). This distinction is important for determining when qasr begins.

Key Terms
ربض (rabad): The suburb or outskirts of a city, consisting of houses and dwellings adjacent to the main city.
فناء (fina'): The courtyard or open space attached to a building or town.
غلوة (ghalwa): A bowshot, a unit of distance approximately 300-400 steps (about 250-330 meters).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The houses and the attached courtyard (fina')
C) The houses, courtyard, and the suburb (rabad)
D) Any point beyond the city limits

Key Takeaway
A traveler in the Hanafi school must pass the houses, attached courtyard, and suburb of his city to qualify for qasr, with an exception if the courtyard is separated by a bowshot.

Conditions for Valid Intention of Travel: Independence, Adulthood, and Minimum Duration
Hanafi conditions for the intention of travel to be valid for shortening prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid travel intention in the Hanafi school and identify whether a person is a follower (tabi') who must rely on the principal's intention.

Core Idea
A valid intention for shortening prayer requires independence, adulthood, and at least three days journey; followers depend on their principal's intention.

This lesson examines the Hanafi conditions for the intention of travel to be legally valid for qasr (shortening prayer). We explore the three prerequisites and how follower status (tabi') affects one's ruling. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly applying the rukhsah of qasr and avoiding invalid prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
From the passage, list the three conditions: independence in ruling (istiqlal bil-hukm), adulthood (bulugh), and minimum duration of three days (muddat thalathati ayyam).
These are cumulative; all must be met for the intention to be valid.
2
Distinguish follower types
Review the examples of followers given: wife, slave, soldier (if receiving stipend), employee, student, captive, coerced person, blind person with a leader. For each, note whose intention controls.
The blind person is a special case: his own intention counts, while the leader's role is merely physical assistance.
3
Determine the follower's ruling
A follower's intention for qasr is derived from the principal. If the follower does not know the principal's intention, he must ask. Once known, he follows the principal's ruling.
If the principal is traveling, the follower shortens; otherwise, he completes the prayer.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Hanifa holds that a wife can prevent her husband from intercourse and demand dower if unpaid, thus she is not a follower until paid. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree.
This difference affects whether she is considered a follower at all.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a 12-year-old boy traveling with his father for 4 days. Can he shorten? No, because he is not an adult. Consider a wife who knows her husband is traveling for 3 days: she may shorten.
Practise with various follower types and conditions.

Commentary
This passage outlines three essential conditions for a valid intention to travel that permits shortening prayer (qasr): 1) Independence – the traveler must not be a follower (tabi') in his ruling; 2) Adulthood – a child's intention is not valid; 3) Minimum duration of three days – if the intended journey is less than three days, qasr is not allowed. The passage then discusses followers (tabi'): a wife is a follower of her husband (with conditions on dower), a slave of his master, a soldier of his commander (if he receives stipend), an employee of his employer, a student of his teacher, a captive of his captor, the coerced of his coercer, and a blind person of his leader (the blind person's own intention counts). If the follower does not know the principal's intention, he must ask; if the principal is traveling, the follower shortens, otherwise not. The commentary also notes that for some rulings like missing Friday prayer or tayammum, even a short journey suffices. There is also an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that a wife cannot prevent her husband even if the dower is unpaid. The student must understand that the conditions are for the validity of the travel intention itself, not for the physical act of traveling.

Key Terms
استقلال بالحكم (istiqlal bil-hukm): Being independent in one's own ruling, not a follower of another in the decision to travel.
تابع (tabi'): A person who is dependent on another in travel, like a wife with husband or slave with master.
مدة ثلاثة أيام (muddat thalathati ayyam): The minimum travel duration for which qasr is allowed; if less, the traveler does not shorten.

Accidental Completion of Prayer by a Traveler
Ruling on accidental completion of prayer by traveler

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a traveler who accidentally completes four rak'ahs, applying the condition of the first sitting and the exception of intending residence.

Core Idea
If a traveler sits for tashahhud, the extra rak'ahs become nafl; without it, the prayer is invalid unless residence is intended.

Imagine you are traveling and lose count, praying four rak'ahs instead of two. Is your prayer valid? The Hanafi school has a detailed ruling based on whether you performed the first sitting (tashahhud) and whether you intended to stay in that place. This lesson unpacks that ruling, showing how the school balances obligation, forgetfulness, and change of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
A traveler must shorten (qasr) four-rak'ah prayers to two. The act in question is accidentally completing four. The default: qasr is a determined obligation (azimah), so completing four is a violation unless specific conditions are met.
Qasr is wajib, not merely sunnah.
2
Check if the first sitting (qu'ud awwal) was performed
If the traveler sat for tashahhud after two rak'ahs before standing for the third, the prayer is valid. The first two rak'ahs count as fard, and the last two become nafl. This is because the obligatory sitting occurred in its proper place.
The first sitting is the place where tashahhud is recited.
3
Apply the consequence of omitting the first sitting
If the traveler did not sit at all after two rak'ahs (i.e., stood up immediately for the third), the prayer is invalid. Reason: he omitted an obligatory sitting (wajib) and mixed nafl with fard before completing the fard.
This invalidation is batil, not just fasid.
4
Recognize the exception: intending residence during prayer
Even without the first sitting, if the traveler intends to reside in that place (valid residence intention) before completing the third rak'ah, his prayer becomes valid. He becomes a resident (muqeem) by intention, so the four rak'ahs are now his complete fard. The missed first sitting is not fatal because it is wajib, not rukn.
Bringing the case of 'intending residence' as a cure for the defect.
5
Determine the need for sajdah sahw
If the prayer is valid (with first sitting) and he was forgetful, sajdah sahw is required due to delaying the obligatory taslim. If deliberate, the prayer is valid but makruh tahrimi with sin.
The delay of the greeting from its proper place triggers compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقصر عزيمة (عندنا لما قدمناه فإذا أتم الرباعية و الحال أنه (قعد القعود الأول) قدر التشهد (صحت صلاته لوجود الفرض في محله وهو الجلوس على الركعتين وتصير الأخريان نافلة له مع الكراهة لتأخير الواجب، وهو السلام عن محله إن كان عامداً فإن كان ساهياً يسجد للسهو ((وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن قد جلس قدر التشهد على رأس الركعتين الأوليين (فلا تصح صلاته لتركه فرض الجلوس في محله واختلاط النفل بالفرض قبل كماله (إلا) إذا نوى الإقامة لما قام للثالثة في محل تصح الإقامة فيه لأنه صار مقيماً بالنية فانقلب فرضه أربعاً، وترك واجب القعود الأول لا يفسد وكذا لو قرأ في ركعة لأنه أمكنه تدارك فرض القراءة في الأخريين بنية الإقامة ) ولا يزال) المسافر الذي استحكم سفره بمضي ثلاثة أيام مسافراً (يقصر حتى يدخل مصره) يعني وطنه الأصلي (أو ينوي إقامته نصف شهر ببلد أو (قرية قدره ابن عباس وابن عمر رضي الله عنهما وإذا لم يستحكم سفره
And shortening is a determined obligation (according to us, as we have presented). So if he completes the four-rak'ah prayer while he sat the first sitting to the extent of tashahhud, his prayer is valid because the obligatory act occurred in its place, which is sitting after two rak'ahs, and the last two become supererogatory for him, with dislike due to delaying the obligatory (i.e., the greeting from its place) if he was deliberate, and if he was forgetful, he performs prostration of forgetfulness. And otherwise (i.e., if he did not sit to the extent of tashahhud after the first two rak'ahs), then his prayer is invalid because he left the obligatory sitting in its place and mixed supererogatory with obligatory before its completion. Except if he intended residence when he stood for the third rak'ah in a place where residence is valid, because he became a resident by intention, so his obligatory prayer turned into four, and leaving the obligatory first sitting does not invalidate. Similarly, if he recited in a rak'ah because he could make up the obligatory recitation in the last two with the intention of residence. And the traveler whose travel has become established by the passage of three days remains a traveler; he shortens until he enters his town, meaning his original homeland, or intends to reside for half a month in a city or village—as estimated by Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them. And if his travel has not become established...
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya continues with rulings on shortening prayer. It states that shortening (qasr) is a determined obligation (azimah) in the Hanafi school, not a concession (rukhsah). If a traveler accidentally completes four rak'ahs, the ruling depends on whether he performed the first sitting (tashahhud). If he sat for the tashahhud, the prayer is valid: the first two rak'ahs count as obligatory, and the last two become supererogatory (nafl) with dislike for delaying the obligatory taslim. If he is deliberate, he sins; if forgetful, he must do sajdah sahw. If he stands up without sitting for tashahhud, the prayer is invalid because he omitted an obligatory sitting and mixed nafl with fard before completion. However, if during the prayer he intends residence (iqamah) before sitting in the third rak'ah, his prayer becomes complete (four rak'ahs) because the intention of residence transforms him into a resident (muqeem), and the obligatory becomes four. In that case, the missed first sitting is not fatal, and he can make up recitation in the remaining rak'ahs. The passage then discusses the traveler status: once travel is established by covering three days' distance, he remains a traveler until he enters his original homeland (watan asli) or intends to stay in a city or village for half a month (15 days). This is based on the opinion of Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar. If the travel is not yet established (i.e., less than three days), the ruling differs (and is covered later).

Key Terms
عزيمة (azimah): A determined obligation, i.e., shortening prayer is originally wajib for the traveler, not a concession.
القعود الأول (al-qu'ud al-awwal): The first sitting after two rak'ahs in a prayer of four rak'ahs, where tashahhud is recited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A traveler mistakenly prays four rak'ahs and did not sit for tashahhud after two rak'ahs. During the third rak'ah, he intends to stay in the city. What is the ruling?
A) The prayer is invalid because he omitted the first sitting.
B) The prayer is valid because the intention of residence makes him a resident, and the four rak'ahs become his fard.
C) The prayer is valid but he must repeat it.
D) The prayer is invalid because travel is still not established.

Key Takeaway
A traveler's accidental completion of four rak'ahs is valid only if he performed the first sitting or intended residence during the prayer.

Duration of Travel and Becoming a Resident
Ruling on when a traveler becomes a resident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for a traveler becoming a resident (15 days) and identify the evidence from the Companions.

Core Idea
A traveler remains a traveler until he either reaches his homeland or intends to stay 15 days in a place.

How long must you plan to stay in a city to stop shortening your prayer? The Hanafi school sets the minimum at 15 days, based on the practice of the Companions. This lesson clarifies that rule and the proof behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default traveler status
A person whose travel has become established (three days' distance) is a traveler and must shorten prayers.
Travel is established by covering the distance of three days (about 48 miles/77 km).
2
Determine what ends traveler status
Traveler status ends when he enters his original homeland (watan asli) or intends to stay for half a month (15 days) in a city or village.
Entering his town is immediate; intention to stay 15 days makes him muqeem from that intention.
3
Recognize the source of the 15-day rule
The Hanafi school relies on the opinion of Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar, who estimated the stay that breaks travel as half a month. Their opinion takes the weight of a hadith in legal matters.
The text says 'قدره ابن عباس وابن عمر' – they estimated it.
4
Compare with other schools
The Shafi'i school requires four days (excluding entry and exit). The difference leads to practical consequences for when a traveler must pray full prayers.
The Hanafi position is more stringent (longer duration).

Source Text

So if he completes the four-rak'ah prayer while he sat the first sitting to the extent of tashahhud, his prayer is valid because the obligatory act occurred in its place, which is sitting after two rak'ahs, and the last two become supererogatory for him, with dislike due to delaying the obligatory (i.e., the greeting from its place) if he was deliberate, and if he was forgetful, he performs prostration of forgetfulness. And otherwise (i.e., if he did not sit to the extent of tashahhud after the first two rak'ahs), then his prayer is invalid because he left the obligatory sitting in its place and mixed supererogatory with obligatory before its completion. Except if he intended residence when he stood for the third rak'ah in a place where residence is valid, because he became a resident by intention, so his obligatory prayer turned into four, and leaving the obligatory first sitting does not invalidate. Similarly, if he recited in a rak'ah because he could make up the obligatory recitation in the last two with the intention of residence. And the traveler whose travel has become established by the passage of three days remains a traveler; he shortens until he enters his town, meaning his original homeland, or intends to reside for half a month in a city or village—as estimated by Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them. And if his travel has not become established...
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya continues with rulings on shortening prayer. It states that shortening (qasr) is a determined obligation (azimah) in the Hanafi school, not a concession (rukhsah). If a traveler accidentally completes four rak'ahs, the ruling depends on whether he performed the first sitting (tashahhud). If he sat for the tashahhud, the prayer is valid: the first two rak'ahs count as obligatory, and the last two become supererogatory (nafl) with dislike for delaying the obligatory taslim. If he is deliberate, he sins; if forgetful, he must do sajdah sahw. If he stands up without sitting for tashahhud, the prayer is invalid because he omitted an obligatory sitting and mixed nafl with fard before completion. However, if during the prayer he intends residence (iqamah) before sitting in the third rak'ah, his prayer becomes complete (four rak'ahs) because the intention of residence transforms him into a resident (muqeem), and the obligatory becomes four. In that case, the missed first sitting is not fatal, and he can make up recitation in the remaining rak'ahs. The passage then discusses the traveler status: once travel is established by covering three days' distance, he remains a traveler until he enters his original homeland (watan asli) or intends to stay in a city or village for half a month (15 days). This is based on the opinion of Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar. If the travel is not yet established (i.e., less than three days), the ruling differs (and is covered later).

Key Terms
نصف شهر (nisf shahr): Half a month, i.e., 15 days, the minimum duration of intention for a traveler to become a resident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When he intends to stay for half a month in a city or village.
B) When he has stayed 15 days in a city.
C) When he enters any city.
D) When he passes through a village.

Key Takeaway
A traveler must intend to stay half a month (15 days) in a place to become a resident and pray full prayers.

Ruling on a Traveler Completing Four Rak'ahs
Ruling on validity of traveler completing four rak'ahs with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a traveler completing four rak'ahs, distinguish between the two scenarios (sitting vs not sitting), and explain the role of iqama intention in transforming the prayer.

Core Idea
For a traveler, completing four rak'ahs without the first sitting invalidates the prayer unless iqama is intended.

In the Hanafi school, shortening (qasr) is the original ruling (azima) for travelers, not a concession. This lesson examines the consequences when a traveler chooses to complete the full four rak'ahs. Al-Tahtawi clarifies two scenarios: if he sits for tashahhud after two rak'ahs, the prayer is valid but disliked; if he does not sit, it is invalid unless he intended to stay (iqama) at the critical moment. Understanding this distinction is essential for every traveler who prays.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
Recognize that the traveler is performing a four-rak'ah obligatory prayer (Zuhr, Asr, or Isha) while on a journey that permits qasr.
The traveler's default obligation is two rak'ahs; completing four is a deviation.
2
Check the First Scenario: Sitting After Two
Determine whether the traveler sat for the tashahhud after the first two rak'ahs. If yes, the prayer is valid because the fard (sitting) was fulfilled. The last two become nafl with karaha (dislike) due to delaying the wajib of salam.
Karaha is tanzihi (slightly disliked), not tahrimi.
3
Check the Second Scenario: No Sitting
If the traveler did not sit after two rak'ahs, the prayer is invalid because he omitted the fard sitting and mixed nafl with fard before completing the fard.
This is the default ruling unless the exception applies.
4
Identify the Exception: Intention of Iqama
If, when standing for the third rak'ah, the traveler genuinely intended to stay (iqama) in a place where iqama is valid, his prayer becomes a four-fard prayer for a resident, and the missed first sitting (wajib) does not invalidate it.
The intention must be sincere, not merely to save the prayer.
5
Consider the Cross-Madhhab Perspective
Compare with the Shafi'i school where the first sitting is sunnah. For them, a traveler completing four without sitting is valid but loses a sunnah.
This ikhtilaf affects practical rulings for followers of different schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He knew that it is valid in the most correct view, and shortening is a resolution (according to us as we have presented). So if he completes the four-rak'ah prayer while he is (traveling) and he sat the first sitting to the extent of tashahhud, his prayer is valid because the obligatory action occurred in its place, which is sitting after two rak'ahs, and the last two become nafl for him with dislike due to delaying the obligatory, which is the greeting from its place if he was deliberate, and if he was forgetful, he prostrates for forgetfulness. Otherwise, i.e., if he had not sat to the extent of tashahhud at the end of the first two rak'ahs, his prayer is not valid because he left the obligatory sitting in its place and mixed nafl with obligatory before its completion, unless he intended iqama when he stood for the third (rak'ah) in a place where iqama is valid, because he becomes a resident by intention, so his obligatory prayer becomes four, and leaving the wajib of the first sitting does not invalidate. Similarly, if he recited in a rak'ah, because he could catch up the obligatory recitation in the last two with the intention of iqama.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage from Al-Tahtawi's Hashiya explains the ruling on a traveler who completes four rak'ahs instead of shortening. The passage first states that shortening (qasr) is a resolution (azima) in the Hanafi school, not a concession. Then it details two scenarios: if the traveler sat for tashahhud after two rak'ahs, the prayer is valid but the last two become nafl with karaha because he delayed the wajib of taslim; if he did not sit, the prayer is invalid due to mixing nafl with fard before completing the fard, unless he intended to stay (iqama) when standing for the third rak'ah. If he intends iqama, his prayer becomes four fard, and missing the first sitting (wajib) does not invalidate. The commentary emphasizes that the intention of iqama must be genuine, not just for completion. The relied-upon position (asahh) is that the prayer is valid with sitting. This is a typical Hanafi ruling on the obligation of sitting after two rak'ahs.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah prayer to two rak'ahs while traveling.
عزيمة (azima): A primary ruling that is obligatory without alternative; here, shortening is the original ruling for travel.
القعود الأول (al-qu'ud al-awwal): The first sitting after completing two rak'ahs in a four-rak'ah prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His prayer is valid but with dislike (karaha).
B) His prayer is invalid unless he intended iqama when standing for the third rak'ah.
C) His prayer is valid and the last two become nafl.
D) His prayer is invalid regardless of intention.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a traveler completing four rak'ahs is valid with karaha if he sits after two, but invalid if he does not sit unless he intended iqama, because the first sitting is fard.

Conditions for a Traveler to Become a Resident (Muqim)
Defines when a traveler remains a musafir and when he becomes a muqim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions that end the travel concession and apply the 15-day iqama rule, citing the proof from the Companions.

Core Idea
Intention to stay 15 days or entering one's hometown stops travel concessions.

Travelers (musafir) may shorten prayers until they become residents. Hanafi fiqh defines residency by either entering one's original home (watan) or intending to stay half a month (15 days) in a place. This lesson unpacks the conditions, proofs, and edge cases from the Hashiya al-Tahtawi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggers for ending travel
The text gives two conditions: (1) entering one's home city (watan), or (2) intending to stay half a month (15 days) in a town or village. Both make the traveler a muqim.
2
Understand the proof from the Companions
The ruling is based on the athar of Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar (ra) who said that if you intend to stay 15 nights, complete your prayer; if uncertain, shorten. This is considered a marfu' in legal estimation.
3
Apply the 15-day rule to various scenarios
The hashia discusses edge cases: staying in multiple locations with overnight intention, staying in a desert (valid if sufficient resources), and staying in an army camp in enemy territory (does not count as iqama).
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that 4 days of stay (excluding entry/exit) makes one a resident. The Hanafi reliance on 15 days is based on transmitted reports (sam'), not opinion.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Tahtawi's Hashiya explains the conditions under which a traveler remains a musafir and when he becomes a muqim. The matn states that a traveler shortens prayer until he enters his hometown or intends to stay half a month (15 days) in a place. The hashia clarifies that travel becomes 'established' after three days' journey. The proof comes from the sayings of Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar, who said that if you intend to stay 15 days, complete your prayer; if unsure, shorten. This is considered like marfu' in legal estimation. It also covers specific cases of niyyah for iqama in multiple locations, deserts, and army camps.

Key Terms
استحكم السفر (istihkam al-safar): Travel becomes 'established' when the journey reaches three days (approx. 48 miles or ~77 km) from one's starting point.
إقامة نصف شهر (iqamat nisf shahr): Intention to stay half a month (15 days) in one place, which converts a musafir into a muqim.
أهل الأخبية (ahl al-akhbiya): Nomads who live in tents (khubā') in the desert; their place of residence is considered habitable for iqama.
