Conditions for the Valid Intention of Iqama (Settling) in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on validity of iqama intention in various locations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a traveler's intention of iqama is valid, and apply them to different scenarios of location and intent.

Core Idea
The validity of iqama intention hinges on the stability of the location and the clarity of the traveler's resolve.

In Hanafi fiqh, a traveler who intends to settle (iqama) in a place for at least 15 days becomes a resident (muqim) and must pray full prayers. However, the intention alone is not sufficient; specific conditions must be met. This lesson from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya clarifies these conditions for various situations, including returning home, indefinite stays, living in multiple towns, deserts, and war zones. Mastering these nuances enables you to correctly determine the prayer obligation of a traveler.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for each scenario
Read the passage and list the five main scenarios: (1) intending to return home before 3 days, (2) intending to stay less than half a month or no fixed intention, (3) intending iqama in two towns, (4) intending iqama in a desert, (5) army in dar al-harb. For each, state whether iqama is valid or not.
2
Determine the underlying condition
For each scenario, identify the key condition that makes iqama valid or invalid. For example, for the desert, the condition is whether the person is a nomad (ahl al-akhbiya) or not.
The condition often relates to the suitability of the location for permanent residence.
3
Analyze the rationale
Understand why the condition matters. E.g., in dar al-harb, the army's situation is unstable, so iqama is invalid.
4
Apply to a practice case
Take a new scenario, e.g., a student living in a city for two years but always intending to leave next month. Determine his status.
Use the ruling on indefinite stay.

والأخبية جمع خباء بغير همز مثل كساء، وأكسية بيت من وبر أو صوف والمراد ما هو أعم من ذلك وأما أهل الأخبية فتصح نيتهم الإقامة في الأصح في مفازة (ولا تصح نية الإقامة العسكرنا بدار الحرب) ولو المخالفة حاصروا، ومصر حالهم بالتردد بين القرار والفرار (ولا تصح نية الإقامة لعسكرنا عن والخروج كذا في التبيين اهـ قوله (النقضه السفر) أي بإرادة الرجوع. قوله: (لأنه ترك) أي لأن نقض السفر ترك والتروك تحصل بمجرد النية قوله : (لأن علقمة الخ) وكذا روي عن ابن عمر، وسعد بن أبي وقاص، وابن عباس رضي الله عنهم. قوله : (لم يعين المبيت بإحداهما) أما إذا عينه بأن نوى أن يقيم الليل في إحداهما ويخرج بالنهار إلى الموضع الآخر فإذا دخل أولاً الموضع الذي عزم على الإقامة فيه بالنهار لم يصر . مقيماً أي حتى يدخل الموضع الذي نوى المبيت فيه، وإن دخل أوّلاً الموضع الذي عزم على الإقامة فيه بالليل صار مقيماً، ثم بالخروج إلى الموضع الآخر لم يصر مسافراً لأن موضع إقامة المرء حيث يبيت فيه، ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت لشخص : أين تسكن؟ يقول : في محلة كذا وهو بالنهار يكون بالسوق نقله السيد العلامة مسكين قوله : ولا تصح نية الإقامة في مفازة مثلها الجزيرة، والبحر، والسفينة، والملاح مسافر، وسفينته ليست بوطن إلا عند الحسن نقله السيد عن البحر . قوله : (وأما أهل الأخبية فتصح نيتهم الإقامة الخ) أي إذا كان عندهم من الماء والكلأ ما يكفيهم تلك المدة، وأهل الأخبية هم الأعراب والترك، والكرد الذين يسكنون المفازة نهر، وقيد بهم لأن غيرهم لو نوى الإقامة معهم لا يصير مقيماً عند الإمام، وهو الصحيح، وعن الثاني روايتان. قوله: لعسكرنا بدار الحرب) أما من دخلها بأمان، ونوى الإقامة في موضعها صحت ويتم درر . قوله: (لمخالفة حالهم أي لعزيمتهم بسبب التردد لأن احتمال وصول مدد إلى العدو ووجود من القليل يغلب بها الكثير قائم، وذلك يمنع قطع القصد فلم تكن دار إقامة . قوله: (في حال محاصرة أهل البغي ولو في المصر كما أفاده أكمل الدين في العناية، وصاحب البحر، والتقييد بغير المصرفي عبارة البعض اتفاقي والبغاة قوم خرجوا عن طاعة الإمام الحق ظانين أنهم على الحق، ولا يحكم بفسقهم لأنهم متمسكون بشبهة، وإن كانت فاسدة فإن لم تكن لهم مكيدة
That if he intended to return to his homeland before the passage of three days, he prays fully (yutimmu) merely by returning, even if he has not reached his homeland, because he breaks the travel. (The act of) travel is not established by mere intention until he actually travels, because it is an action. And (if) he intends less than that, i.e., less than half a month, or does not intend anything, and remains like that for years, intending to leave tomorrow or after a week, then Alqama ibn Qays stayed like that in Khwarazm for two years shortening the prayer. And the intention of iqama is not valid in two towns without specifying the overnight stay in one of them, and each is independent. If one is subordinate, like a village in which Friday is obligatory, iqama is valid upon entering either, and similarly if he specifies the overnight stay in one of the two towns, because iqama is attached to the place of overnight stay. And the intention of iqama is not valid in a desert (mafaza) for other than the people of tents (ahl al-akhbiya), due to the lack of suitability of the place for them. Tents (akhbiya) is the plural of khuba' without hamz, like bisa' and aksiya, a house of wool or hair, and the meaning is more general. As for the people of tents, their intention of iqama is valid in the most correct view in a desert. And the intention of iqama is not valid for our army in a war zone (dar al-harb), even if they are besieging a city, because their situation is one of hesitation between staying and fleeing. And the intention of iqama is not valid for our army...
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage from Al-Tahtawi's Hashiya covers several specific rulings regarding the intention (niyyah) of iqama. First, it addresses the case of a traveler who intends to return home before three days: he becomes a muqim merely by the intention to return, even before actually reaching home, because breaking travel is a cessation (tark) and cessations are effected by mere intention. Second, if a traveler intends to stay less than half a month, or has no fixed intention, he remains a musafir even if he stays for years, like Alqama ibn Qays who stayed in Khwarazm for two years shortening his prayer. Third, the ruling on niyyah in two towns: if the towns are independent, the intention is valid only if he specifies where he will sleep (mabit); if one is a suburb that gathers for Friday, it is valid. Fourth, in a desert (mafaza), iqama is invalid for settled people because the place is not suitable, but it is valid for nomads (ahl al-akhbiya) who live there. Fifth, for an army in a war zone (dar al-harb), iqama is not valid even if they besiege a city, because their position is unstable and they may retreat. The hashia also discusses the condition of the army when besieging rebels (baghi) within an Islamic city, where the ruling is similar due to uncertainty.

Key Terms
مفازة (mafaza): A desert or barren land where habitation is not established except for nomads.
أهل الأخبية (ahl al-akhbiya): People who live in tents (al-khubā') made of wool or hair, i.e., nomads.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): A non-Muslim territory where war is allowed; for a Muslim army in it, iqama is not valid due to uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is the intention of iqama valid for a Muslim army besieging a city in a war zone?
A) Yes, if they intend to stay for 15 days.
B) No, because their state is one of hesitation between staying and fleeing.
C) Yes, if they have taken the city.
D) No, because they are not allowed to pray in war.

Key Takeaway
The validity of iqama intention depends on the stability of the location and the clarity of the traveler's resolve; mere duration of stay is insufficient without fulfilling these conditions.

Intention to Return and Prolonged Stay
Explanation of when a traveler becomes resident by intention to return and the permissibility of prolonged qasr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a traveler may shorten prayer indefinitely and explain why naqd al-safar occurs by intention alone.

Core Idea
Intention to return (naqd al-safar) is a cessation achieved by mere intention, unlike travel which requires action.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a common confusion: how long can a traveler shorten prayer when far from home? The ruling hinges not on the duration of stay but on the intention to leave. If the traveler intends to leave before half a month (fifteen days)—or has no fixed intention at all—they remain a traveler even if they stay for years. The key principle is that breaking travel (naqd al-safar) is a cessation, which occurs by mere intention, while initiating travel requires physical movement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of naqd al-safar
Recognize that naqd al-safar is the legal act of breaking one's travel status. According to the passage, it occurs by merely intending to return to one's homeland, without needing to physically move.
This is distinct from the act of traveling itself.
2
Distinguish travel from cessation
Travel (safar) is an action that requires physical movement to begin. In contrast, breaking travel is a cessation (tark), which is achieved by mere intention. This is the core usul principle applied here.
Cessations are easier to effect than actions in Hanafi legal theory.
3
Identify the two scenarios for qasr
The passage states that a traveler may shorten prayer if (a) he intends to stay less than half a month, or (b) he has no specific intention of stay. In both cases, he remains a traveler and may shorten, even if he stays for years.
"Half a month" is fifteen days in the Hanafi school.
4
Recognize the dalil from companion practice
The proof cited is the practice of 'Alqama ibn Qays, who stayed in Khwarazm for two years shortening prayer, and similar reports from Ibn 'Umar, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and Ibn 'Abbas. This shows that prolonged stay with the intention to leave does not invalidate qasr.
This is a qawl sahabi, which is a source of law in the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a traveler who has no fixed return date and stays for three years, he may continue to shorten prayer because his intention is not to reside for half a month or more. The key is the intention, not the actual duration.
If he later decides to stay for half a month or more, he becomes a resident from that moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن أراد الرجوع لوطنه قبل مضي ثلاثة أيام يتم بمجرد الرجوع، وإن لم يصل لوطنه لنقضه السفر لأنه ترك بخلاف السفر لا يوجد بمجرد النية حتى يسير لأنه فعل وقصر إن نوى أقل منه أي من نصف شهر (أو لم ينو) شيئاً (ويقي) على ذلك (سنين) وهو ينوي الخروج في غد، أو بعد جمعة لأنّ علقمة بن قيس مكث كذلك بخوارزم سنتين يقصر الصلاة قوله (النقضه السفر) أي بإرادة الرجوع. قوله: (لأنه ترك) أي لأن نقض السفر ترك والتروك تحصل بمجرد النية قوله : (لأن علقمة الخ) وكذا روي عن ابن عمر، وسعد بن أبي وقاص، وابن عباس رضي الله عنهم.
That if he intends to return to his homeland before three days have passed, he becomes a resident upon mere return. If he does not reach his homeland, he breaks his travel because it is a cessation; unlike travel which does not occur by mere intention until he travels because it is an action. And he may shorten if he intends less than half a month, or if he does not intend anything, and he remains in that state for years while intending to leave tomorrow or after a Friday, because 'Alqama ibn Qays stayed like that in Khwarazm for two years shortening prayer. His statement (breaking travel): meaning by intending to return. His statement (because it is cessation): meaning breaking travel is cessation and cessations occur by mere intention. His statement (because 'Alqama etc.): and similar is narrated from Ibn 'Umar, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and Ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the traveler's intention to return and its effect on the permissibility of shortening prayer. The author explains that if a traveler intends to return to his homeland before three days elapse, he becomes a resident (mutimm) immediately upon that intention, even without physically returning. Conversely, if he does not intend to return, he remains a traveler and may shorten prayer as long as his intention to leave is less than half a month, or if he has no specific intention. The proof is the precedent of 'Alqama ibn Qays and other Companions who stayed in a place for years with the intention to leave and still shortened prayer. The commentary clarifies that breaking travel (naqd al-safar) is an act of cessation, which is achieved by mere intention, unlike travel itself, which requires physical movement.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah prayer to two rak'ahs while traveling.
نية الإقامة (niyyat al-iqama): Intention to stay in a place for a period that makes one a resident, requiring full prayer.
نقض السفر (naqd al-safar): Breaking the legal status of travel by intending to return to one's homeland.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a traveler intends to stay in a city for less than half a month but ends up staying for two years, what is the ruling on his prayer?
A) He must perform full prayers because he stayed long.
B) He may shorten prayer because his intention was less than half a month.
C) He becomes a resident after one year.
D) He must perform qada for the shortened prayers.

Key Takeaway
A traveler remains a traveler as long as he does not intend to stay for half a month or more, even if he stays for years, because breaking travel is by intention alone.

Intention of Residence in Two Towns: The Mabit Criterion
Clarification of when intention to reside in two places is valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid intention of residence in two towns and apply the Hanafi rule that residence is determined by the place of sleep (mabit) to determine whether a person is a resident or traveler.

Core Idea
Residence is where you sleep, not where you spend the day.

This lesson examines a subtle issue affecting travel salah (qasr): what if a person intends to reside in two different towns? The Hanafi school resolves this by focusing on where one sleeps at night. We'll unpack the ruling from the matn and its commentary, learning how the 'mabit' criterion determines residency status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and general ruling
The act is making an intention (niyyah) to take up residence (iqamah) in two different towns. The general rule: such an intention is not valid unless the place of night-time sleeping (mabit) is specified.
2
Distinguish independent vs. dependent towns
If the towns are independent (each a separate legal locality), the mabit must be specified. If one town is a dependent suburb (tabi'ah) of the other, such that Friday prayer is obligatory on its residents in the main town, then entering either town suffices for residency.
Dependency means the suburb is treated as part of the main town for travel rulings.
3
Apply the mabit criterion
If the person specifies sleep in Town A and day in Town B, he becomes a resident only upon entering Town A (the sleep location). Leaving to Town B during the day does not break residency.
Residence is attributed to the place of mabit, as the commentary explains with the analogy of a person who lives in a neighborhood but works in the market.
4
Determine when residency begins
When entering the sleep town first: immediate residency. When entering the day town first: no residency until entering the sleep town.
This affects when qasr is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح نية الإقامة ببلدتين لم يعين المبيت بإحداهما وكل واحدة أصل بنفسها، وإذا كانت تابعة كقرية يجب على ساكنها الجمعة تصح الإقامة بدخول أيتهما وكذا تصح إذا عين المبيت بواحدة من البلدتين لأن الإقامة تضاف لمحل المبيت قوله (لم يعين المبيت بإحداهما) أما إذا عينه بأن نوى أن يقيم الليل في إحداهما ويخرج بالنهار إلى الموضع الآخر فإذا دخل أولاً الموضع الذي عزم على الإقامة فيه بالنهار لم يصر مقيماً أي حتى يدخل الموضع الذي نوى المبيت فيه، وإن دخل أوّلاً الموضع الذي عزم على الإقامة فيه بالليل صار مقيماً، ثم بالخروج إلى الموضع الآخر لم يصر مسافراً لأن موضع إقامة المرء حيث يبيت فيه، ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت لشخص : أين تسكن؟ يقول : في محلة كذا وهو بالنهار يكون بالسوق نقله السيد العلامة مسكين
And intention of residence in two towns is not valid if he does not specify sleep in one of them, each being independent in itself. If one is dependent like a village where Friday attendance is obligatory, residence is valid by entering either. Similarly, it is valid if he specifies sleep in one of the two towns because residence is attributed to the place of sleep. His statement (did not specify sleep in one of them): if he specifies by intending to spend the night in one and go out during the day to the other, then if he first enters the place where he intends to stay during the day, he does not become a resident until he enters the place where he intends to sleep. If he first enters the place where he intends to sleep, he becomes a resident. Then by going out to the other place, he does not become a traveler because a person's residence is where he sleeps. Do you not see that if you ask a person where he lives, he says in such and such neighborhood, while during the day he is in the market. Transmitted by al-Sayyid al-'Allamah Miskin.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This is a ruling statement on the validity of intending residence in two towns. The matn states that intention of residence is invalid if one does not specify which town he will sleep in at night. However, if one town is a suburb (dependent) where Friday prayer is obligatory, simply entering either suffices for residency. The commentary explains that if one specifies sleep in one town, he becomes resident upon entering that town, and leaving to the other town during the day does not break residency. The principle is that residence is determined by where one sleeps at night, analogous to a person who works in the market but lives in a neighborhood.

Key Terms
مبيت (mabit): The place where one sleeps at night, determining the location of residence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person intends to reside in City A during the day and sleep in City B at night. When does he become a resident?
A) When he enters City A.
B) When he enters City B.
C) Only after spending one night in City B.
D) He never becomes a resident.

Key Takeaway
The decisive factor for residence in the Hanafi school is where one sleeps at night, not where one spends the day.

Intention of Residence in a Desert
Who can establish residence in an uninhabited area

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intention of residence in a desert and distinguish between those for whom it is valid and those for whom it is not.

Core Idea
The desert can be a home only for those who live in tents – residence depends on the suitability of the place to one's lifestyle.

In Islamic law, the concept of 'residence' (iqama) affects obligations like shortening prayers. This ruling addresses whether a person can intend to become a resident in a desert (mafaza). The key is whether the place is suitable for settlement based on one's normal dwelling. The passage distinguishes between tent dwellers (ahl al-akhbiya) and others, and clarifies that even for tent dwellers, sufficient resources are needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'intention of residence' (niyyat al-iqama) in a desert. The ruling is that it is not valid for non-tent dwellers.
2
Understand the condition for validity
Validity depends on the person being from 'ahl al-akhbiya' – people who normally live in tents. For them, the desert is a suitable place.
Tent dwellers include Bedouins, Turks, Kurds.
3
Note the additional condition for tent dwellers
Even for tent dwellers, the intention is valid only if they have sufficient water and pasture for the intended duration.
This condition is from the commentary.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that non-tent dwellers cannot become residents even if they stay with tent dwellers. From Abu Yusuf, there are two narrations.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa.
5
Apply to analogous cases
The same principle applies to islands, ships, and seas. A sailor's ship is not a homeland except according to al-Hasan al-Basri.
These analogies are from the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح نية الإقامة في مفازة لغير أهل الأخبية لعدم صلاحية المكان في حقه والأخبية جمع خباء بغير همز مثل كساء، وأكسية بيت من وبر أو صوف والمراد ما هو أعم من ذلك وأما أهل الأخبية فتصح نيتهم الإقامة في الأصح في مفازة قوله : ولا تصح نية الإقامة في مفازة مثلها الجزيرة، والبحر، والسفينة، والملاح مسافر، وسفينته ليست بوطن إلا عند الحسن نقله السيد عن البحر . قوله : (وأما أهل الأخبية فتصح نيتهم الإقامة الخ) أي إذا كان عندهم من الماء والكلأ ما يكفيهم تلك المدة، وأهل الأخبية هم الأعراب، والترك، والكرد الذين يسكنون المفازة نهر، وقيد بهم لأن غيرهم لو نوى الإقامة معهم لا يصير مقيماً عند الإمام، وهو الصحيح، وعن الثاني روايتان
And intention of residence in a desert is not valid for other than people of tents (ahl al-akhbiya) because the place is not suitable for them. 'Akhbiya' is the plural of 'khiba' without hamza, like 'kisa'' (garment) and 'aksiya' (clothing), a house made of fur or wool, and the intended meaning is broader. As for people of tents, their intention of residence in a desert is valid in the most correct view. His statement: 'and intention of residence in a desert' – similar are an island, sea, ship, and the sailor is a traveler, and his ship is not a homeland except according to al-Hasan, transmitted by al-Sayyid from al-Bahr. His statement: 'As for people of tents, their intention of residence is valid' – i.e., if they have enough water and pasture for that period. People of tents are the Bedouins, Turks, and Kurds who live in the desert. He specified them because if others intend residence with them, they do not become residents according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and this is correct; from the second (Abu Yusuf) there are two narrations.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that establishing residence in a desert (mafaza) depends on the person's lifestyle. People who normally live in tents (Bedouins, Turks, Kurds) can intend residence there because the desert is their natural habitat. For others, the desert is not a place suitable for settlement, so they cannot become residents there and must remain travelers. The same applies to ships and islands; a sailor's ship is not a homeland. The commentary adds that for tent dwellers to be considered residents, they must have sufficient water and pasture for the intended stay. If a non-tent dweller resides with them, he does not become a resident according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
أهل الأخبية (ahl al-akhbiya): People who live in tents (khiba'), typically Bedouins, Turks, and Kurds who inhabit deserts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A city-dweller intends to reside in a desert for two weeks. According to the passage, what is the ruling?
A) He becomes a resident immediately.
B) He does not become a resident and remains a traveler.
C) He becomes a resident only if he stays over a month.
D) It depends on his intention.

Key Takeaway
The validity of intending residence in a desert depends on whether the person's lifestyle makes the desert a suitable home; only tent dwellers qualify, and even they need sufficient resources.

Army's Intention of Residence in Enemy Territory and During Siege
When an army’s intention of residence is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the intention of residence for an army in enemy territory and during siege of rebels, identify the exceptions, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
An army’s intention to reside is invalid due to uncertainty; soldiers remain travelers, but a single entrant with aman may become resident.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi addresses a specific fiqh question: when can soldiers on campaign become muqim (resident) for prayer? The Hanafi ruling is that the army's intention of residence is invalid in enemy territory and even when sieging rebels in their own land, because their presence is uncertain. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its rationale, and an intra-Hanafi difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is making niyyah al-iqamah (intention of residence) for an army. The ruling is that it is invalid (batil).
2
Understand the two scenarios
(a) army in enemy territory (dar al-harb) even if besieging; (b) army in their own territory (dar al-Islam) while besieging rebels (ahl al-baghy).
3
Grasp the rationale (talil)
The reason is the uncertainty of the situation (taraddud) due to possible military reversal, which prevents firm intention (qat' al-qasd).
This uncertainty is the core illa behind the ruling.
4
Note the exception
One who enters enemy territory with aman (safe passage) can make valid intention of residence and becomes muqim.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference
Zufar differentiates, and a detail from Abu Yusuf is narrated, but the relied-upon view is the invalidity.
The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn) is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح نية الإقامة العسكرنا بدار الحرب) ولو المخالفة حاصروا، ومصر حالهم بالتردد بين القرار والفرار (ولا تصح نية الإقامة لعسكرنا بدارنا في حال محاصرة أهل البغي للتردد كما ذكرنا ولو كانت الشوكة ظاهرة لنا عليهم قوله لعسكرنا بدار الحرب) أما من دخلها بأمان، ونوى الإقامة في موضعها صحت ويتم درر . قوله: (لمخالفة حالهم أي لعزيمتهم بسبب التردد لأن احتمال وصول مدد إلى العدو ووجود من القليل يغلب بها الكثير قائم، وذلك يمنع قطع القصد فلم تكن دار إقامة . قوله: (في حال محاصرة أهل البغي ولو في المصر كما أفاد أكمل الدين في العناية، وصاحب البحر، والتقييد بغير المصرفي عبارة البعض اتفاقي والبغاة قوم خرجوا عن طاعة الإمام الحق ظانين أنهم على الحق، ولا يحكم بفسقهم لأنهم متمسكون بشبهة، وإن كانت فاسدة فإن لم تكن لهم شبهة فهم لصوص أي قطاع طريق قهستاني من بحث البغاة. قوله (ولو كانت الشوكة ظاهرة لنا عليهم للعلة السابقة وفصل زفر وتفصيله رواية عن الثاني
And intention of residence for our army in enemy territory is invalid, even if they are besieging the opposing force, because their situation is wavering between settling and fleeing. And intention of residence for our army in our territory while besieging rebels (ahl al-baghy) is invalid due to the uncertainty as we mentioned, even if our power is apparent over them. His statement: 'for our army in enemy territory' – but one who enters with aman (safe passage) and intends residence there, his intention is valid and he becomes a resident (yutimm). His statement: 'due to their situation' – i.e., due to their determination because of uncertainty, because the possibility of enemy reinforcements arriving and the existence of a small group being overcome by a larger one is present, and that prevents firm intention, so it is not a place of residence. His statement: 'in the case of besieging rebels' – even if in a city (misr), as Akmal al-Din affirmed in al-'Inayah and the author of al-Bahr. The restriction to non-cities in some expressions is accidental. Rebels are those who rebel against the rightful imam, thinking they are right; they are not judged as sinners because they hold a semblance of evidence, even if corrupt. If they have no semblance, they are thieves (highway robbers) as in Qahistani's commentary on rebels. His statement: 'even if our power is apparent over them' – due to the same cause. Zufar differentiates, and his detail is narrated from the second (Abu Yusuf).
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of intention of residence for military personnel. An army in enemy territory cannot become resident because their presence is uncertain; they may have to fight or flee. Even in their own territory while sieging rebels (ahl al-baghy), the uncertainty prevents them from establishing residence. An exception is one who enters enemy territory with aman (safe passage) and intends to stay; he can become resident. The commentary explains that the reason is the possibility of military reversal, which prevents firm intention (qat' al-qasd). Rebels are distinguished from thieves by their belief that they are right; if they have no semblance (shubha), they are like highway robbers. Zufar has a differing opinion.

Key Terms
أهل البغي (ahl al-baghy): Rebels who revolt against the rightful imam under a semblance of justification; not judged as sinners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid; he prays full prayers.
B) Invalid; he remains a traveler and shortens prayer.
C) Valid only if he is an officer.
D) Invalid only if the siege lasts less than a month.

Key Takeaway
An army’s intention to reside is invalid when there is uncertainty due to military conflict, even if they seem to have the upper hand.

Ruling on Traveler Following Resident in Prayer
Validity of prayer when a traveler follows a resident or vice versa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a traveler may follow a resident imam and derive the ruling using the Hanafi position and its proof.

Core Idea
A traveler follows a resident within time completes four; after time invalid; resident can always follow traveler.

This passage from Maraqi al-Falah with its hashya discusses a nuanced issue in congregational prayer: the validity of a traveler following a resident imam and vice versa. Understanding these rulings requires careful consideration of time, obligation, and the strength of the imam's prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, note when a traveler follows a resident (within prayer time vs. after time). Second, note when a resident follows a traveler (always valid).
2
Examine the condition of time
For the first scenario, the ruling hinges on whether the traveler began following within the prayer time. Even if time exits later, it's valid if started within time.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The Prophet (ﷺ) prayed with the people of Makkah while traveling and said 'Complete your prayer, for we are a traveling people.' This shows that a resident can follow a traveler, and the traveler's obligation is not diminished.
The hadith is cited as the primary evidence.
4
Understand the lahiq ruling
If a traveler follows a resident and joins late (after the first rak'ah), he is a lahiq and does not recite Quran in the missed rak'ahs to avoid redundancy.
This is a detail from the commentary.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a hypothetical fact pattern, determine whether the prayer is valid based on these rules.
This step synthesizes the lesson.

وإن اقتدى مسافر بمقيم يصلي رباعية، ولو في التشهد الأخير (في الوقت صح) اقتداؤه (وأتمها أربعاً) تبعاً لإمامه واتصال المغير بالسبب الذي هو الوقت ولو خرج الوقت قبل إتمامه، أو ترك الإمام القعود الأول في لو كانت فائتة في حق الإمام مؤداة في حق المأموم كما إذا كان المأموم يرى قول الإمام في الظهر، والإمام يرى قولهما وقول الشافعي فإنه يجوز دخوله معه في الظهر بعد المثل قبل المثلين كما في السراج . قوله : (لأن فرضه لا يتغير بعد خروجه) فكان اقتداء المفترض بالمتنفل في حق القعدة إن كان الإبتداء في الشفع الأول، أو في حق القراءة إن كان الاقتداء في الشفع الثاني، أو في حق التحريمة كما في السراج عن الحواشي لأن قوله : (لأنه صلى الله عليه وسلم الخ ولأن صلاة المسافر في الحالين أقوى، وبناء الضعيف على القوي جائز . قوله : ( أتموا صلاتكم) روي أن أبا يوسف لما حج مع هارون الرشيد، وصلى بالناس ركعتين بمكة قال : أتمو And if a traveler follows a resident who is praying a four-rak'ah prayer, even in the last tashahhud, during the time period his following is valid and he completes four rak'ahs following his imam, and connection of the exception (qasr) to the cause which is time. Even if the time exits before completion, or the imam abandons the first sitting, in the correct view. After the time exits, his following is not valid, even if the resident's ihram was before time exit, because his obligation does not change after its exit. And the reverse: if a resident follows a traveler, following is valid in both cases (within time and after its exit) because he (the Prophet) prayed with the people of Makkah while he was a traveler and said: 'Complete your prayer, for we are a traveling people.' And his sitting is a stronger obligation than the first sitting for the resident. The followers complete individually without recitation and without prostration of forgetfulness. Commentary explains various conditions and proofs.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This ruling discusses the validity of a traveler following a resident imam and vice versa. Within the prayer time, a traveler may validly follow a resident and must complete four rak'ahs. The traveler's original obligation (qasr) is overridden by following. After time expires, following is invalid for the traveler because the resident's obligation has been fulfilled. Conversely, a resident may follow a traveler anytime because the traveler's prayer is considered a valid act of worship and the resident's obligation is stronger. The proof is the Prophet's action in Makkah. The commentary adds that if the traveler follows in the second rak'ah, he is a 'lahiq' and omits recitation to avoid redundancy.

Key Terms
لاحق (lahiq): A person who joins the congregation after the first rak'ah of the imam and makes up missed rak'ahs after the imam's salam.
مسافر (musafir): A traveler who is eligible to shorten four-rak'ah prayers to two.
مقيم (muqim): A resident who prays the full prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid.
B) The traveler must repeat the prayer.
C) The prayer is valid and he completes four rak'ahs.
D) The traveler must shorten to two rak'ahs.

Key Takeaway
A traveler may follow a resident only if he begins within the prayer time; a resident may always follow a traveler.

Rulings on a Traveler Following a Resident Imam and Vice Versa
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a traveler following a resident imam and a resident following a traveler imam, explain the condition of time, and apply the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
A traveler following a resident imam must complete four rak'ahs if he joins within time; a resident following a traveler imam is valid at any time.

This lesson examines two related scenarios: a traveler (musafir) joining a resident (muqim) imam, and a resident joining a traveler imam. The rulings hinge on the time of joining and the nature of the prayer. We will explore the textual proof from the Sunnah and the legal reasoning behind the validity of building the weak on the strong.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage contrasts two cases: a traveler following a resident imam, and its opposite (a resident following a traveler imam). For each, note the ruling within and after the prayer time.
The case of traveler following resident is more restricted.
2
Analyze the condition of time
For the traveler following a resident, the key condition is that the traveler's joining (ihram) must occur within the time of that prayer. If the time has expired, the following is invalid even if the imam started in time.
The reasoning: the traveler's obligation does not change after the time exits.
3
Examine the proof
The hadith of the Prophet (PBUH) when he prayed as a traveler in Makkah and told the residents to complete their prayer provides the basis. The imperative 'a'timmū' indicates obligation for the residents to complete, and the Prophet's own shortening as a traveler is the precedent.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (qati' al-dalala).
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi commentary
The commentary addresses nuances: even if the imam omits the first sitting, the follower's prayer is valid in the soundest view. Also, the lahiq (one who caught the beginning) does not recite nor perform sajdah sahw in the part he completes alone, and others cannot follow him.
These details stem from the principle that the follower's obligation is tied to the imam's.
5
Derive the principle of building weak on strong
In the reverse case (resident following traveler), the validity at any time is justified by the principle that the stronger prayer (the traveler's shorter prayer) can accommodate the weaker (the resident's full prayer). The resident completes alone after the imam's salams.
This is based on the Prophet's action and statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اقتدى مسافر بمقيم يصلي رباعية، ولو في التشهد الأخير (في الوقت صح) اقتداؤه (وأتمها أربعاً) تبعاً لإمامه واتصال المغير بالسبب الذي هو الوقت ولو خرج الوقت قبل إتمامه، أو ترك الإمام القعود الأول في الصحيح (وبعده) أي بعد خروج الوقت (لا يصح) اقتداؤه المسافر بالمقيم، ولو كان إحرام المقيم قبل خروج الوقت لأن فرضه لا يتغير بعد خروجه (وبعكسه) بأن اقتدى مقيم بمسافر (صح) الاقتداء (فيهما) أي في الوقت وفيما بعد خروجه لأنه صلى بأهل مكة وهو مسافر وقال : أتموا صلاتكم فإنا قوم سفر، وقعوده فرض أقوى من الأول في حق المقيم، ويتم المقيمون منفردين بلا قراءة ولا سجود سهو،

قوله: (يصلي رباعية) الجملة صفة مقيم قال السيد ولا حاجة إليه لعلمه من قوله وأتمها أربعاً قوله: (ولو في التشهد) متعلق بقوله اقتدى كقوله في الوقت. قوله : ( في الوقت ولو قدر تحريمة في الأصح قهستاني . قوله : (ولو خرج الوقت) مبالغة على قوله صح . قوله : (أو) ترك الإمام القعود الأول) لأن القعدة صارت واجبة في حقه أيضاً، فلا يبطل فرضه بتركها، وعليه الفتوى نهر . قوله : (لا يصح اقتداء المسافر بالمقيم) مقيد بكونها فائتة في حق الإمام والمأموم أما لو كانت فائتة في حق الإمام مؤداة في حق المأموم كما إذا كان المأموم يرى قول الإمام في الظهر، والإمام يرى قولهما وقول الشافعي فإنه يجوز دخوله معه في الظهر بعد المثل قبل المثلين كما في السراج . قوله : (لأن فرضه لا يتغير بعد خروجه) فكان اقتداء المفترض بالمتنفل في حق القعدة إن كان الإبتداء في الشفع الأول، أو في حق القراءة إن كان الاقتداء في الشفع الثاني، أو في حق التحريمة كما في السراج عن الحواشي لأن تحريمة الإمام اشتملت على فرض، ونقل، وتحريمة المقتدي اشتملت على الفرض فقط فكانت أقوى اهـ وفيه أن تحريمة المسافر مشتملة على نحو التسبيح، والتكبير وإن أراد من جهة القراءة فيرجع إلى ما ذكره صاحب الهداية. قوله : (لأنه صلى الله عليه وسلم الخ ولأن صلاة المسافر في الحالين أقوى، وبناء الضعيف على القوي جائز . قوله : ( أتموا صلاتكم) روي أن أبا يوسف لما حج مع هارون الرشيد، وصلى بالناس ركعتين بمكة قال : أتمو صلاتكم فإنا قوم سفر فقال له واحد منهم : نحن أعلم بهذا منك فقال له أبو يوسف : لو علمت ما تكلمت في الصلاة فقال :هرون ولو كان مثل هذا الجواب بدلاً عن الملك الذي أعطانيه الله تعالى لكنت أسر بذلك كذا في الشرح قوله: (فإنا قوم سفر) يستعمل سفر مفرداً، وجمعاً قال رجل ،سفر، وقوم سفر، والمراد هنا الجمع ذكره العلامة نوح. قوله : (أقوى من الأول) أي من القعود الأول. قوله : (بلا قراءة) في الأصح لأنهم لاحقون حيث أدركوا أول صلاتهم مع الإمام، وفرض القراءة قد تأدى فيتركونها احتياطاً كذا في الهداية،

والكافي قوله : (ولا سجود سهو) لو سهواً فيما يتمون لأنهم كاللاحقين. قوله: (ولا يصح الاقتداء بهم) لأنم بالاقتداء التزموا الموافقة في الركعتين فينفردون في الباقي إلا أنهم مقتدون تحريمة لا فعلاً
And if a traveler follows a resident imam who prays a four-rak'ah prayer, even in the last tashahhud, within the time his following is valid, and he completes it as four due to his imam, and the connection of the changer to the cause which is the time, even if the time exits before his completion, or the imam leaves the first sitting in the sound view. And after it (i.e., after the time exits) his following is not valid, even if the resident's ihram was before the time exits because his fard does not change after its exit. And its opposite: if a resident follows a traveler, the following is valid in both (i.e., in the time and after its exit) because he (the Prophet) prayed with the people of Makkah while he was a traveler and said: Complete your prayer, for we are a traveling people. And his sitting is a stronger fard than the first with respect to the resident, and the residents complete individually without recitation and without prostration of forgetfulness. ... He said: (He prays four) the clause is an adjective for resident; ... He said: (Without recitation) in the soundest view because they are lahiqun having caught the beginning of their prayer with the imam, and the obligation of recitation has been fulfilled, so they leave it as a precaution. ... And al-Kafi: He said: (And no prostration of forgetfulness) if they forget in what they complete because they are like lahiqun. He said: (And it is not valid to follow them) because by following they committed to conformity in the two rak'ahs, so they become individual in the rest, except that they are followers in tahrima, not in action.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage begins with the ruling on a traveler following a resident imam. The matn states that if a traveler (musafir) follows a resident (muqim) imam in a four-rak'ah prayer, it is valid only if the traveler's joining occurs within the time of that prayer, even if he joins during the final tashahhud. In this case, he must complete the full four rak'ahs due to following the imam. The condition is that the traveler must be within the prayer time when he makes the intention (tahrima). If the time expires before he completes or if the imam omits the first sitting (qa'dah), the following is still valid in the soundest view. However, if he joins after the time has ended, it is not valid, even if the imam's ihram was during the time, because the traveler's obligation (fard) does not change after the time expires. The commentary then explains nuances: the ruling is qualified for prayers that are qada (make-up) for both imam and follower; if only the imam is making up, it may be allowed under some views. Then the opposite case: a resident following a traveler imam is valid in both time and after time, because the Prophet (PBUH) prayed as a traveler in Mecca and told the residents to complete their prayer, indicating that building the weak (resident's full prayer) on the strong (traveler's shorter prayer) is permissible. The commentary includes a historical anecdote about Abu Yusuf and Harun al-Rashid. Further, it discusses that the muqtadi (follower) in this case is a lahiq (one who caught the first part of the prayer with the imam) and thus does not recite in the part he completes alone, nor does he perform sajdah sahw (prostration of forgetfulness). Finally, it is not valid for others to follow these muqtadis when they complete alone because they are followers in tahrima but not in action.

Key Terms
مسافر (musafir): A traveler who has set out on a journey of at least three days (approx. 48 miles) and intends to travel beyond the residential area.
مقيم (muqim): A resident who does not qualify as a traveler; one who is settled in his hometown or intends to stay more than 15 days.
لاحق (lahiq): A follower who caught the beginning of the prayer with the imam but then separated for a valid reason, still considered as having followed the imam in the first part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A traveler joins a resident imam for Zuhr prayer within the Zuhr time. According to the Hanafi school, how many rak'ahs must the traveler pray?
A) 2 rak'ahs (as a traveler)
B) 4 rak'ahs (following the imam)
C) 3 rak'ahs
D) It depends on the traveler's intention

Key Takeaway
The validity of a traveler following a resident imam hinges on joining within the prayer time; in the reverse, the resident's following is always valid due to the principle of building the weak on the strong.

The Traveler Imam's Statement and the Muqtadi's Recitation
Ruling on recommended statement for traveler imam and muqtadi's prohibition from reciting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for the lahiq and masbuq followers regarding recitation after a traveler imam, and state the recommended action for the imam.

Core Idea
A resident follower behind a traveler imam must not recite when completing prayer, because the obligation is already fulfilled.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi clarifies two important rulings in congregational prayer. First, the recommended practice for a traveler imam to inform residents to complete their prayer. Second, the critical distinction between a lahiq (follower who caught the beginning) and a masbuq (latecomer) regarding recitation. Understanding this distinction prevents incorrect application of the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage covers (1) the imam's recommended statement 'Complete your prayer' after salams, and (2) the muqtadi's prohibition from reciting when completing his prayer after the traveler imam finishes.
2
Understand the reasoning for the imam's statement being mandub
It is not obligatory because the imam's situation is not fixed; he could be asked before prayer, or the followers might complete after him. The soundest view is that it is said after both salams.
Some say after the first, but the soundest is after both.
3
Recognize the key distinction: lahiq vs masbuq
The lahiq caught the first part of the prayer with the imam, so the obligation of recitation (qira'ah) is already fulfilled. The masbuq missed at least one rak'ah and did not hear the imam's recitation, so he must recite in his missed rak'ahs.
The text contrasts the muqtadi who is a lahiq with the masbuq.
4
Apply the reasoning to the muqtadi's ruling
For the lahiq muqtadi, precaution dictates not reciting because it is either prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) or recommended; leaving it is safer. For the masbuq, recitation is obligatory (fard) because the obligation was not fulfilled.
The text says the lahiq's recitation oscillates between prohibition and recommendation, so precaution is to leave it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وندب للإمام) بعد التسليمتين في الأصح ، وقيل بعد التسليمة الأولى أن يقول أتموا صلاتكم فإني (مسافر) كما روينا، وإنما كان مندوباً لأنه لم يتعين مصرفاً لحال الإمام لجواز السؤال قبل الصلاة، أو بعد إتمامهم صلاتهم (وينبغي أن يقول لهم الإمام (ذلك قبل شروعه في الصلاة لدفع الاشتباه ابتداء (ولا يقرأ) المؤتم (المقيم فيما يتمه بعد فراغ إمامه المسافر في (الأصح لأنه أدرك مع الإمام أوّل صلاته وفرض القراءة قد تأدى بخلاف المسبوق

قوله : (وقيل بعد التسليمة الأولى) خوف إفسادهم صلاتهم بالتسليمة الثانية لأنهم لا ينتظرون شيئاً بخلافه بعد التسليمة الأولى. قوله : ( في الأصح) وقال بعض المشايخ يقرأ كالمسبوق. قوله : ( لأنه أدرك الخ) بيانه أنه لما كان لاحقاً كان خلف الإمام حكماً فكان مقتدياً به من هذا الوجه، ه، وهو منفرد حقيقة فبالنظر إلى أنه مقتد تكره له القراءة تحريماً، وبالنظر إلى أنه منفرد تستحب له القراءة، إذ فرض القراءة قد تأدى في الشفع الأول، وإذا دار الأمر بين الحرمة، والندب فالاحتياط هو الترك فكان جعل مقتدياً أولى من جعله منفرداً بخلاف المسبوق فإنه أدرك قراءة نافله فلم يسقط فرض القراءة عنه، فدارت قراءته بين أن تكون مكروهة تحريماً، أو ركنا تفسد الصلاة بتركه فكان الاحتياط في حقه القراءة فصار جعله منفرداً أولى من جعله مقتدياً فكانت قراءته فيما يقضى فرضاً
And it is recommended for the imam after the two salams, in the soundest view, and it is said after the first salaam, to say: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler' as we have narrated. And it is only recommended because it is not specified to the imam's situation, due to the permissibility of asking before the prayer, or after they complete their prayer. And it is proper for the imam to say that before beginning the prayer to remove confusion from the start. And the muqtadi (resident) does not recite in what he completes after the departure of his traveling imam in the soundest view, because he caught with the imam the beginning of his prayer and the obligation of recitation has been fulfilled, unlike the masbuq. ... He said: (And it is said after the first salaam) for fear of corrupting their prayer by the second salaam because they are not waiting for anything, unlike after the first salaam. He said: (In the soundest view) and some mashayikh say he recites like the masbuq. He said: (Because he caught...) its explanation: when he is a lahiq, he is behind the imam in ruling, so he is following him from this perspective, while he is actually individual. So from the perspective that he is a follower, recitation is disliked prohibitively; from the perspective that he is individual, recitation is recommended. Since the obligation of recitation has been fulfilled in the first pair, and when the matter oscillates between prohibition and recommendation, precaution is to leave it, so considering him a follower is more appropriate than considering him individual, unlike the masbuq, who caught the supererogatory recitation so the obligation of recitation is not lifted from him. So his recitation oscillates between being prohibitively disliked or a pillar that invalidates the prayer if left. So precaution for him is to recite, so considering him individual is more appropriate than considering him a follower, so his recitation in what he makes up is obligatory.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage addresses the recommended (mandub) action for the traveler imam to inform the residents that he is traveling and that they should complete their prayer. It is recommended after both salams or after the first. The imam should preferably say this before starting the prayer to avoid confusion. Then the ruling on the muqtadi (follower) who is a resident: when the traveler imam finishes, the muqtadi does not recite (qira'ah) in the rak'ahs he completes alone, because he is considered a lahiq (one who caught the first part with the imam) and the obligation of recitation has already been fulfilled in the first two rak'ahs. The commentary contrasts this with the masbuq (latecomer) who must recite because he did not catch the recitation with the imam. The detailed reasoning is given, balancing the prohibition of recitation out of caution.

Key Terms
مندوب (mandub): An action that is recommended but not obligatory; performing it is rewarded, omitting it is not sinful.
مسبوق (masbuq): A follower who missed one or more complete rak'ahs with the imam and must make them up after the imam's salam.
لاحق (lahiq): A follower who caught the beginning of the prayer with the imam and continues after the imam's salam as an individual, but is still considered a follower in some rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) After the first salaam
C) After both salams (soundest view)
D) Only after the second salaam

Key Takeaway
The crucial distinction between lahiq and masbuq determines whether a follower must recite when completing a shortened prayer behind a traveler imam.

End-of-Time Determinant for Qada Rak'ahs
Ruling with proof on the determinant of qada rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the number of rak'ahs in qada of a missed prayer is determined by the state at the end of the prescribed time.

Core Idea
The qada of a missed prayer mirrors the state at the end of the time, not the state during the time.

Many students assume that the qada of a missed prayer follows the traveler's or resident's state at the moment the prayer was missed. This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi clarifies a critical Hanafi principle: the end of the prescribed time is the decisive factor. This seemingly small detail has wide implications for many practical scenarios, including changes in travel status, eligibility (e.g., conversion), and exemptions due to sickness. We will unpack this principle step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the missed prayer and its time frame
First, determine which obligatory prayer was missed (e.g., Zuhr, Asr). Each prayer has a fixed time window. The 'end of the time' is the moment when the time expires for that specific prayer.
For example, the end of Zuhr time is when the shadow of an object becomes equal to its height plus the shadow at noon (according to Hanafi).
2
Determine the state at the end of that time
At the exact moment the prayer time ends, was the person a traveler (safar) or a resident (muqim)? Also note any changes in eligibility (puberty, Islam, sanity, purity).
Traveler here means fulfilling the conditions of shar'i travel (distance, etc.). Residence includes both one's homeland and temporary stay (watan iqama).
3
Apply the ruling: 2 rak'ahs for traveler, 4 for resident
If at the end of the time the person was a traveler, the qada is 2 rak'ahs. If a resident, 4 rak'ahs. The actual state at the time of missing is irrelevant.
This is based on the rational proof (dalil aqli) that the obligation is established at the end of the time when there is no performance.
4
Distinguish from other cases: sickness, eligibility, and original homeland
The passage also notes exceptions: a sick person makes up missed prayers based on ability at the time of qada, not end of time. Eligibility changes (e.g., conversion) at the end make the prayer obligatory. The original homeland (watan asli) is only nullified by another original homeland, not by temporary residence.
These points are distinct rulings but relate to the same principle of 'end of time' being the reference point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفائتة السفر و فائتة الحضر تقضي ركعتين وأربعاً) فيه لف ونشر مرتب لأن القضاء بحسب الأداء بخلاف فائتة المريض والقوي فإن المريض إذا برىء يقضي بالركوع والسجود وإذا مرض يقضي بالإيماء فائتة الصحة لسقوط الركوع والسجود بالعذر ولزومهما بالقدرة حال القضاء والمعتبر فيه أي لزوم الأربع بالحضر والركعتين بالسفر (آخر الوقت فإن كان في آخره مسافراً صلى ركعتين، وإن كان مقيماً صلى أربعاً لأنه المعتبر في السببية عند عدم الأداء فيما قبله من الوقت فتلزمه الصلاة لو صار أهلاً لها في آخر الوقت ببلوغ وإسلام وإفاقة من جنون، وإغماء وطهر من حيض ونفاس وتسقط بفقد الأهلية فيه بجنون، وإغماء ممتد ونفاس وحيض ويبطل الوطن الأصلي بمثله فقط) أي لا يبطل بوطن

والمراد بالوطن الأصلي وطن الاستيطان الذي خرج منه للسفر، فإنه لا يبطل إلا بوطن مثله، أي بوطن استيطان آخر.
And the missed prayer of travel and the missed prayer of residence are made up as two and four rak'ahs respectively, in it there is arranged wrapping and spreading because the making up is according to the performance, unlike the missed prayer of the sick and the strong. For if the sick person recovers, he makes up with bowing and prostration; and if he becomes sick, he makes up with gestures for the missed prayer of health, because bowing and prostration are dropped due to the excuse, and they become obligatory with ability at the time of making up. And the consideration in it, i.e., the obligation of four in residence and two in travel, is the end of the time. For if at its end he is a traveler, he prays two; if a resident, he prays four, because it is what is considered in causality when there is no performance in the part of time before it. So the prayer becomes obligatory on him if he becomes eligible for it at the end of time by reaching puberty, embracing Islam, waking from insanity, unconsciousness, and purification from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. And it falls due to loss of eligibility in it by insanity, prolonged unconsciousness, postpartum bleeding, and menstruation. And the original homeland is only nullified by its like, i.e., it is not nullified by a homeland [of temporary residence]. And what is meant by the original homeland is the homeland of settlement from which he left for travel, for it is only nullified by a homeland like it, i.e., by another homeland of settlement.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage deals with the qada of missed prayers. The principle is that the number of rak'ahs in qada is determined by the state of the person at the end of the prescribed time. For example, if a person missed a Zuhr prayer while traveling, he makes it up as two rak'ahs because at the end of Zuhr time he was a traveler. Similarly, if he was a resident, he makes up four. The reasoning is that the obligation of prayer is established at the end of the time. This also applies to eligibility conditions: if a person becomes eligible (e.g., embraces Islam) at the last moment of the time, the prayer is obligatory on him for that time. If eligibility is lost (e.g., prolonged insanity), the prayer is not obligatory. The passage also discusses the case of illness: the sick person, upon recovery, must make up missed prayers with full actions (ruku', sujud) because the excuse is gone. Conversely, a healthy person who becomes ill must make up with gestures. The end of the time is the critical factor in determining the number of rak'ats. Finally, the ruling on original homeland (watan asli) is that it is only nullified by another original homeland, not by a temporary residence.

Key Terms
فائتة (fā'ita): A missed obligatory prayer that must be made up (qada).
وطن أصلي (watan asli): The original homeland from which a person departs for travel; it is only nullified by another original homeland.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person missed the Zuhr prayer while traveling. After the Zuhr time ended, he arrived home as a resident. How should he make up the Zuhr prayer?
A) Two rak'ahs
B) Four rak'ahs
C) Two if he still feels like traveling
D) He must repeat the prayer as resident

Key Takeaway
The end of the prescribed time determines the number of rak'ahs for qada of a missed prayer.

The Traveler Imam's Recommendation to Inform Followers
Ruling on the imam's statement after taslim and its timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the traveler imam's statement after taslim, explain why it is mandub, and identify the preferred timing.

Core Idea
A traveler imam is recommended, not obligated, to tell followers to complete their prayer; best said before starting to avoid confusion.

When a traveler leads residents in prayer, should the imam inform them before or after? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: it is recommended (mandub) but not required. The timing is also refined: the preferred view is after both taslims, but the imam should ideally announce it before beginning to prevent doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the traveler imam saying to the followers after taslim: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler.' The ruling is mandub (recommended), not wajib, as explicitly stated by the word 'وندب'.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites a narration ('كما روينا') as evidence. Though the exact hadith is not quoted, it refers to prophetic practice. The recommendation is derived from this narration combined with the principle of preventing confusion (دفع الاشتباه).
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
Why is it mandub? Because the imam's situation is not fixed; followers could ask before prayer or after completing. Thus, informing is recommended but not essential for validity.
4
Refine the timing
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: after the first taslim or after both. The most correct view (الأصح) is after both taslims. Additionally, it is befitting (ينبغي) for the imam to announce before starting to remove confusion from the outset.
The 'ينبغي' indicates a higher level of recommendation, though the core ruling remains mandub.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وندب للإمام) بعد التسليمتين في الأصح ، وقيل بعد التسليمة الأولى أن يقول أتموا صلاتكم فإني (مسافر) كما،روينا، وإنما كان مندوباً لأنه لم يتعين مصرفاً لحال الإمام لجواز السؤال قبل الصلاة، أو بعد إتمامهم صلاتهم (وينبغي أن يقول لهم الإمام (ذلك قبل شروعه في الصلاة لدفع الاشتباه ابتداء
And it is recommended for the imam, after the two taslims in the most correct view, and it is said after the first taslim, to say: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler' as we have narrated. And it is only recommended because it is not specified for the imam's situation due to the possibility of asking before the prayer or after they complete their prayer. And it is befitting that the imam say that to them before beginning the prayer to remove confusion from the start.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the traveler imam. The text states it is recommended (mandub) for the imam to inform the followers after the taslim (either after both taslims in the most correct view, or after the first) that they should complete their prayer because he is a traveler. The reasoning is that it is not obligatory for the imam to declare his intention; asking is permissible either before prayer or after they finish. However, it is proper (yambaghi) that the imam says it before starting the prayer to avoid confusion. This is from the hashia of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah.

Key Terms
مندوب (mandub): A recommended act, omission of which is not blameworthy but doing it is meritorious.
تسليمة (taslimah): The salutation at the end of prayer; here referring to the first or second taslim.
الأصح (al-asahh): The most correct view among multiple valid opinions within the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Mandub
C) Haram
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
A traveler imam is recommended (mandub) to inform followers to complete their prayer; the most correct timing is after both taslims, and it is even better to announce before beginning.

Resident Follower's Recitation After Traveler Imam
Ruling with proof on lahiq's recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a resident follower's recitation in qada rak'ahs behind a traveler imam and explain the proof based on the lahiq/masbuq distinction.

Core Idea
A resident follower's recitation obligation is fulfilled by the imam; he must not recite in completed rak'ahs.

This lesson addresses a specific congregational scenario: a resident praying behind a traveler imam who shortens the prayer. When the imam makes taslim, the follower must complete the remaining rak'ahs alone. The Hanafi school rules that he does not recite in those rak'ahs, and this ruling hinges on his status as a 'lahiq'—one who joined the prayer from the beginning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the situation: a resident (muqim) follows a traveler imam (musafir) who performs only two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer. After the imam's taslim, the follower stands to complete the remaining rak'ahs.
2
State the ruling
The follower does not recite (Qur'an) in those additional rak'ahs according to the most correct view. Recitation is considered makruh tahrimi.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The proof is that the follower caught the beginning of the prayer with the imam; thus the obligation of recitation (fard al-qira'ah) was already fulfilled during the shared rak'ahs.
The Arabic phrase: 'لأنه أدرك مع الإمام أوّل صلاته وفرض القراءة قد تأدى'.
4
Distinguish from the masbuq
Contrast this with the masbuq (latecomer) who missed the first rak'ah(s) with the imam. The masbuq must recite in his qada rak'ahs because his obligation was not fulfilled.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafi mashayikh analogized the lahiq to the masbuq and said he should recite. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is no recitation.
Al-Tahtawi confirms this as the most correct view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقرأ) المؤتم (المقيم فيما يتمه بعد فراغ إمامه المسافر في (الأصح لأنه أدرك مع الإمام أوّل صلاته وفرض القراءة قد تأدى بخلاف المسبوق
And the resident follower does not recite in what he completes after the departure of his traveler imam in the most correct view, because he caught the beginning of his prayer with the imam and the obligation of recitation has been fulfilled, unlike the one who was preceded (masbuq).
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This ruling concerns the resident follower who prays behind a traveler imam. When the imam makes taslim after two rak'ahs, the follower stands to complete his prayer as four rak'ahs. The question is whether he recites in these additional rak'ahs. According to the most correct view (al-ashahh), he does not recite because he joined the imam at the beginning of the prayer, so the obligation of recitation (fard al-qira'ah) was already fulfilled in the first two rak'ahs they prayed together. This contrasts with the masbuq (latecomer) who missed part of the imam's prayer and must recite in his qada. The reasoning emphasizes that the follower is a 'lahiq' (one who joins from the start) and thus treated as a follower in terms of prohibition of recitation, while also being independent in some aspects. The cautious view (ihtiyat) is to avoid recitation.

Key Terms
لاحق (lahiq): One who joins the imam from the beginning of the prayer.
مسبوق (masbuq): One who joins the imam after the first rak'ah has begun and misses part of the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because he is a lahiq and the obligation of recitation was fulfilled
C) Because recitation is not required in the first two rak'ahs
D) Because he is a traveler himself

Key Takeaway
A resident follower behind a traveler imam does not recite in his qada rak'ahs because, as a lahiq, his recitation obligation is already fulfilled.

Making Up Missed Travel and Residence Prayers
Ruling with proof and contrasting case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of rak'ahs for qada of missed travel and residence prayers and apply the principle that qada follows the original obligation.

Core Idea
Missed travel prayers are made up as 2 rak'ahs, missed residence as 4 – qada mirrors the original.

This lesson clarifies a fundamental rule in Hanafi fiqh: when making up a missed prayer, the number of rak'ahs is determined by the prayer's original obligation, not the current situation. We will see how this applies to travel and residence prayers and contrast it with the case of a sick person, where the manner of performance (ruku/sujud vs. pointing) depends on ability at the time of qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the passage: 'Fā'itat al-safar tuqḍā rak'atayn wa fā'itat al-ḥaḍar tuqḍā arba'an' – the missed travel prayer is made up as 2 rak'ahs, the missed residence as 4.
This is the core ruling.
2
Understand the Proof
The text states: 'al-qaḍā' bi-ḥasab al-adā'' – making up is according to the performance. That is, the missed prayer must be performed exactly as it was obligatory at its time.
This principle applies to the number of rak'ahs.
3
Distinguish from the Sick Person's Case
Contrast: If a sick person recovers, he makes up missed prayers with bowing and prostration. If a healthy person becomes sick, he makes up with pointing (ima'), because ability at qada time matters for the manner, but not for the rak'ah count.
The number of rak'ahs is fixed by the original obligation, not by current ability.
4
Apply the Principle
When a traveler misses a prayer, he must make it up as 2 rak'ahs, even if he later becomes resident. Similarly, a resident missing a prayer makes it up as 4, even if he later travels.
The key is the nature of the missed prayer itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفائتة السفر و) فائتة الحضر تقضي ركعتين وأربعاً) فيه لف ونشر مرتب لأن القضاء بحسب الأداء بخلاف فائتة المريض والقوي فإن المريض إذا برىء يقضي بالركوع والسجود وإذا مرض يقضي بالإيماء فائتة الصحة لسقوط الركوع والسجود بالعذر ولزومهما بالقدرة حال القضاء والمعتبر فيه أي لزوم الأربع بالحضر والركعتين بالسفر
And the missed prayer of travel and the missed prayer of residence are made up as two rak'ahs and four rak'ahs respectively, in ordered inversion. Because making up is according to the performance, unlike the missed prayer of the sick and the strong. For if a sick person recovers, he makes up with bowing and prostration; if he becomes sick, he makes up with pointing for the missed prayer of health due to the dropping of bowing and prostration by excuse and their necessity by ability at the time of making up. And what is considered is the necessity of four in residence and two in travel.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This ruling states that missed prayers are to be made up exactly as they were obligatory at the time of performance. Thus, a missed travel prayer is made up as 2 rak'ahs, and a missed residence prayer as 4 rak'ahs. This is an ordered inversion because the text first mentions the missed prayer of travel then residence, but the explanation matches: travel 2, residence 4. It contrasts with the case of a sick person who recovers: he makes up with full ruku and sujud because the excuse is gone. Conversely, if a healthy person becomes sick, he makes up with ishara (pointing) because the excuse is present at the time of making up. The key principle is that the obligation is determined by the state at the end of the time (akhir al-waqt), as will be discussed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed or invalidated prayer.
إيماء (ima'): Pointing with the head for bowing and prostration when unable.
فائتة (fā'itah): Missed prayer, a prayer that was not performed in its prescribed time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many rak'ahs does one make up for a missed travel prayer?
A) 4
B) 2
C) 3
D) It depends on the time of qada

Key Takeaway
Missed prayers are made up exactly as they were originally obligated: travel prayers as 2 rak'ahs, residence prayers as 4 rak'ahs.

End of Time Determines Prayer Obligation
Ruling: state at end of time determines rak'ahs and obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the state at the end of the prayer time (akhir al-waqt) determines the number of rak'ahs and whether the prayer is obligatory, and explain the rational proof (dalil aqli) behind this principle.

Core Idea
The end of the prayer time is decisive: your status and eligibility at that moment determine the ruling.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi rule: the moment that determines the nature of a prayer obligation is the end of its time. Whether you pray two or four rak'ahs, and whether the prayer is even required, hinges on your condition at akhir al-waqt. The ruling is supported by a rational proof based on the nature of time as the cause (sabab) of the obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the two sub-rulings: (1) the number of rak'ahs depends on travel status at the end of the time, and (2) eligibility (or loss thereof) at the end determines whether the prayer is obligatory.
2
Understand the Rational Proof
Analyze why the end of the time is considered: because the time is the cause (sabab) for the obligation, and when no performance has occurred earlier, the cause fully materializes at the end.
This is a dalil aqli – rational deduction from the definition of sabab.
3
Apply to Eligibility Scenarios
Work through the examples: becoming eligible (puberty, Islam, recovery) at the end makes the prayer obligatory; losing eligibility (insanity, menstruation) at the end drops the obligation.
These are conditions (shurut) that attach at the end.
4
Contrast with Other Schools
Note that other madhhabs (e.g., Shafi'i) may consider the majority of the time rather than the end. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آخر الوقت فإن كان في آخره مسافراً صلى ركعتين، وإن كان مقيماً صلى أربعاً لأنه المعتبر في السببية عند عدم الأداء فيما قبله من الوقت فتلزمه الصلاة لو صار أهلاً لها في آخر الوقت ببلوغ وإسلام وإفاقة من جنون، وإغماء وطهر من حيض ونفاس وتسقط بفقد الأهلية فيه بجنون، وإغماء ممتد ونفاس وحيض
The end of the time: if at the end he is a traveler, he prays two rak'ahs; if at the end he is a resident, he prays four, because it is the consideration in the cause when there is no performance in what preceded of the time. So the prayer becomes obligatory on him if he becomes eligible for it at the end of the time by reaching puberty, becoming Muslim, recovering from insanity, fainting, and becoming pure from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. And it falls by loss of eligibility at that time by insanity, prolonged fainting, postpartum bleeding and menstruation.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This ruling clarifies a key Hanafi principle: the determining factor for whether a prayer is to be performed as 2 or 4 rak'ahs is the state of the person at the end of the prayer time (akhir al-waqt). If at the end of the time the person is a traveler, he prays 2; if a resident, 4. This is because the cause (sabab) for the obligation is the time itself, but the manner is determined by the state at the end when the prayer becomes due. Additionally, if a person becomes eligible (by puberty, Islam, recovery from unconsciousness, etc.) at the end of the time, the prayer becomes obligatory on him. Conversely, if he loses eligibility (by insanity, prolonged fainting, menstruation, etc.) at the end, the prayer falls away. This principle is applied in various scenarios.

Key Terms
آخر الوقت (akhir al-waqt): The end of the prescribed time for a prayer, considered the moment of obligation.
سببية (sababiyyah): Causality; here, the time is the cause for the obligation of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule in this passage, what determines whether a prayer is obligatory when a person becomes eligible (e.g., reaches puberty) at the end of the prayer time?
A) The state at the beginning of the time
B) The state at the end of the time
C) The majority of the time
D) The time of actual prayer

Key Takeaway
The end of the prayer time (akhir al-waqt) is the decisive moment for whether the prayer is obligatory, how many rak'ahs to pray, and whether eligibility triggers or drops the obligation.

Rules of Original Homeland and Residence Homeland
Rulings on invalidation of original and residence homelands and their definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between watan asli and watan iqama, state what invalidates each, and apply these rules to determine whether a traveler prays complete or shortened in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Original homeland is invalidated only by another original; residence homeland is invalidated by its like, travel, or return to original.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the Hanafi rules of homelands (watan), which affect whether a traveler prays 2 or 4 rak'ahs. Understanding these distinctions is essential for determining one's prayer status when moving between multiple homes or residing temporarily. The commentary provides detailed examples and edge cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of homeland
Distinguish between watan asli (original homeland) and watan iqama (residence homeland). Note their definitions: watan asli is where one was born, married, or intends to settle permanently; watan iqama is where one intends to stay fifteen days or more.
Each has different invalidation rules.
2
State the invalidation rule for watan asli
The text says 'لا يبطل الوطن الأصلي إلا بمثله' meaning the original homeland is invalidated only by its like (another original homeland). It is not invalidated by residence homeland or by travel.
This is the key principle.
3
State the invalidation rule for watan iqama
Residence homeland is invalidated by its like (another residence homeland of same duration), by initiating travel after it, or by returning to the original homeland.
These are the three invalidators.
4
Analyze the examples given
Work through the example of an Egyptian who moves to Syria with family and marries there: he now has two original homelands. When traveling, if he enters either, he prays complete. Another example: a person staying at a khanqah for 15 days has a residence homeland; returning to original invalidates it.
Understand how the rules apply in concrete scenarios.
5
Apply the principle to edge cases
Consider edge cases like concubinage (does it create original homeland?) and the scenario of leaving a residence homeland with initial travel intention then passing through again. The commentary notes that if travel is initiated after residence, it invalidates; if travel intention was there before, it may not.
These refine the application.

Source Text
