And the original homeland is invalidated only by its like, meaning it is not invalidated by a residence homeland nor by travel, because a thing is not invalidated by what is less than itself but only by its like or above. And the prior existence of travel is not required to establish an original homeland by consensus, nor for residence homeland in the apparent narration. And if he does not leave his family but acquires a family in another town, his first homeland is not invalidated; each is an original homeland for him. And the residence homeland is invalidated by its like, and also by initiating travel after it, and by returning to the original homeland, due to what we have mentioned. And the original homeland is where a person was born or married, or if he did not marry and was not born there but intends to live without leaving. And the residence homeland is a place suitable for residence as we have mentioned, and he intends to stay there half a month or more. Prayer: if he enters it while a traveler before its invalidation, and the researchers did not consider it a homeland. And the benefit of this is that he prays completely in the place of residence. He said: (by its like) meaning even if there is no travel distance between them, because it is not required for residence homeland in the apparent narration. For example, if he leaves Egypt and stays in Qalyub for a period, it is called a residence homeland, but then no rulings apply. He said: (rather, he acquires a family) and likewise if he acquires a family in three places, the ruling is the same apparently. He said: (by initiating travel after it) even if he returns for a need, he prays short. And 'after it' means after the residence in it, whether he initiated the travel from it or from another place after it; it is not required to be from it, as indicated by the words of the author of al-Nahr in his refutation of al-Zayla'i. And what remains is if he leaves it with the intention of travel first, then passes beyond a travel distance from it or from the original, and did not stay elsewhere, then passes by it, does he pray completely? Apparently, yes, because he did not enter the original nor stay elsewhere nor initiate travel after it. And he said: (due to what we have mentioned) that a thing is invalidated only by its like or above. He said: (or married) consider the ruling if he takes a concubine; if concubinage is considered, the original homeland is more than four. He said: (as we have mentioned) that it must be one, and not a desert, nor a war camp, nor a rebel territory. He said: (and the benefit of this) it is better to mention after what he said, because it is a benefit of what preceded. He said: (and he is a traveler) to exclude the case if he breaks the travel before it becomes established after staying in a place for fifteen days, then he prays completely if he enters it because he becomes a resident by breaking the travel. And the author similar in the commentary on residence homeland, clarifying the principle: for example, an Egyptian who moved with his family to Syria; if he returns as a traveler and enters his Egyptian city, he does not pray complete by mere entry. If he kept his family and also married in Syria, he prays complete upon entering each of the two homelands. And if he leaves intending Syria and intends to stay at the Khanqah al-Siryaqusiyah for fifteen days, his original homeland is not invalidated. So if he returns to it for a need, he prays complete there. Then if he leaves and enters the Khanqah, he prays short because the residence homeland there is invalidated by the original. Likewise, if he leaves the Khanqah after intending fifteen days there, and did not return to his original homeland, and did not intend travel until he reached Bilbays, then intended fifteen days there, the residence homeland at the Khanqah is invalidated. And likewise if he leaves it and intends travel.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This is a detailed discussion of the Hanafi rules regarding homelands (watan) and their effect on prayers. The original homeland (watan asli) is only invalidated by taking another original homeland; it is not invalidated by residence homeland or travel. A residence homeland (watan iqama) is invalidated by another residence homeland of the same duration, by initiating travel, or by returning to the original homeland. The text defines the original homeland as the place where one was born, married, or where one intends to settle permanently. The residence homeland is where one intends to stay fifteen days or more. Numerous examples are given, such as a person moving from Egypt to Syria: he now has two original homelands. When traveling, if he enters one of his original homelands, he prays complete. If he has a residence homeland, it is invalidated by returning to the original. The commentary also addresses edge cases, such as breaking travel before it is established, and the effect of concubinage. The overall principle is that a thing is invalidated only by its like or superior.

Key Terms
وطن أصلي (watan asli): Original homeland where one was born, married, or intends to settle permanently without intention of leaving.
وطن إقامة (watan iqama): Residence homeland where one intends to stay fifteen days or more.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What invalidates a residence homeland (watan iqama)?
A) Only another residence homeland
B) Another residence homeland, initiating travel after it, or return to original homeland
C) Only return to original homeland
D) Travel of any distance

Key Takeaway
The original homeland is only invalidated by another original homeland, while the residence homeland is invalidated by its like, travel initiation, or return to original.

When Standing is Excused: Ta'adhur and Ta'assur
Defining illness and excusing conditions for sitting in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real impossibility (ta'adhur) and legal impossibility (ta'assur) and state the Hanafi conditions that excuse a sick person from standing in prayer.

Core Idea
Severe pain or genuine fear of worsening illness excuses standing — mere discomfort does not.

This lesson opens the chapter on the sick's prayer by defining illness and establishing the key criterion for when standing is waived. The Hanafi school distinguishes between absolute impossibility and severe difficulty, treating the latter as a valid excuse under the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj). We will examine the precise conditions that allow a sick person to sit in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on standing in prayer
Standing (qiyam) is a fard (obligatory pillar) in prayer for those able. This lesson defines when that obligation is lifted for the sick person.
The prayer itself remains wajib; only the requirement of standing is waived.
2
Distinguish between ta'adhur and ta'assur
Ta'adhur is real impossibility: the person physically cannot stand. Ta'assur is legal impossibility: standing is possible but causes severe pain or genuine fear of harm. Both excuse standing, but ta'assur is based on hardship.
Ta'assur is considered hukmi (legal) impossibility, treated like real impossibility in the ruling.
3
List the conditions for ta'assur
The text mentions severe pain (alam shadid) or fear based on prior experience or a trustworthy Muslim doctor's report. The fear must be a strong belief (ghalabat al-zann).
Examples of severe pain: dizziness, toothache, migraine, eye inflammation.
4
Apply the standard of severity
Severity is judged individually, not by a universal measure. The person's own condition and reliable evidence determine whether the pain is severe enough to excuse standing.
This follows the Hanafi principle of 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease).
5
Evaluate a scenario
Given a hypothetical case (e.g., mild backache vs. severe migraine), determine whether standing is excused by applying the conditions from the text.
Mild discomfort or fatigue does not qualify; only severe pain or genuine fear does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter on the prayer of the sick. From the addition of the verb to its subject, and illness is a state of the body outside the natural course. Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the prayer of the sick (Bab Salat al-Mareed). The author first defines illness linguistically and legally: illness is a condition of the body that deviates from its natural state. The key ruling is that if standing becomes absolutely impossible (al-ta'adhur) or extremely difficult (al-ta'assur) due to severe pain (e.g., dizziness, toothache, migraine, or eye inflammation, as in al-Qahistani), or due to a genuine fear of worsening the illness based on prior experience or the report of a trustworthy Muslim doctor, then the sick person is excused from standing in prayer. The text distinguishes between real impossibility (haqiqi) and legal impossibility (hukmi), meaning severe difficulty that is treated as impossibility. The author notes that this applies whether the condition occurs during the prayer or before it. The passage also includes a brief discussion about the traveler's homelands, which seems to be a carryover from the previous section due to OCR merging; the core of this lesson is the definition of illness and the conditions allowing sitting in prayer.

Key Terms
تعذر (ta'adhur): Real impossibility: when the sick person physically cannot stand.
تعسر (ta'assur): Legal impossibility: when standing is possible but causes severe pain or fear of worsening illness.
ألم شديد (alam shadid): Severe pain that prevents the person from standing normally, such as dizziness or toothache.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Being tired from travel

Key Takeaway
The sick person is excused from standing in prayer only when standing is impossible (ta'adhur) or causes severe pain or genuine fear of harm (ta'assur) — mere discomfort is insufficient.

Watan al-Sakan: Definition and Effect on Travel and Qasr Prayer
Critical refinement of watan al-sakan ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define watan al-sakan, state its effect on the traveler's license to shorten prayer, and distinguish between the positions of al-Zaylai and al-Nahr.

Core Idea
A temporary stay of less than 15 days does not end travel status or nullify the license to shorten prayer.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the Hanafi ruling on watan al-sakan, a place where a traveler intends to stay less than half a month. The author first states the basic rule: such a stay does not create a new domicile (watan al-iqamah) nor nullify the traveler's legal status. He then refines this by presenting an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether watan al-sakan can ever affect prayer length. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly determining when a traveler may shorten prayers.

Guided Path
1
Define watan al-sakan
Identify the key elements: it is a place where a traveler intends to stay less than 15 days, and it is not his original homeland (watan asli) or his place of residence (watan iqamah). It does not nullify travel status.
Contrast with watan al-iqamah (15+ days) which does nullify travel.
2
State the basic ruling
From the opening sentence: watan al-sakan does not invalidate watan al-iqamah nor does it nullify the legal rules of travel (safar) such as qasr.
The ruling applies only if the person is already a traveler when entering the place.
3
Analyze the condition 'while he is a traveler'
The commentator notes that this phrase is not a condition (qaid) but a descriptor. Al-Zaylai's scenario shows that if a person goes to a village without intending travel (i.e., he is not a traveler), he must complete prayer. But if he later intends to travel before entering his city or staying 15 days elsewhere, he shortens.
This aligns with the principle that travel status depends on intention and distance.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Zaylai holds that watan al-sakan can have an effect (completing prayer) in certain circumstances. Al-Nahr critiques this, insisting that the traveler always shortens because he remains a traveler. The author (al-Tahtawi) supports al-Nahr's view as the mufta bihi.
The dispute centers on whether a non-travel intention before entry affects later travel.
5
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position: a traveler who enters a place with intention to stay less than 15 days remains a traveler and may shorten prayer. If he leaves for travel, he shortens; if he enters his original homeland or stays 15+ days elsewhere, travel ends.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

السكنى وهو ما أي موضع ( ينوي الإقامة فيه دون نصف شهر) وكان مسافراً فلا يبطل به وطن الإقامة ولا يبطل السفر. قوله: (وكان مسافراً) ليس بقيد وقال الزيلعي : عامتهم على أن وطن السكني يفيد وتتصور تلك الفائدة فيمن خرج إلى قرية لحاجة، ولم يقصد سفراً ونوى أن يقيم بها أقل من نصف شهر يتم، فلو خرج منها لا للسفر، ثم بدا له أن يسافر قبل أن يدخل مصره، وقبل أن يقيم أقل من خمسة عشر يوماً في موضع آخر قصر فلو مر بتلك القرية أتم لأنه لم يوجد منه ما يبطله مما هو فوقه، أو مثله اهـ بتغيير ما وقوله فلو خرج منها لا للسفر قيد به لأنه لو خرج منها للسفر بطل اتفاقاً، وقوله: ثم بدا له أن يسافر قبل أن يدخل مصره، وقبل أن يقيم الخ قيد به لأنه لو دخل مصره لبطل بما فوقه، وهو الوطن الأصلي ولو أقام بمحل أقل من مدة الإقامة لبطل بمثله. قال في النهر وما في الزيلعي : ممنوع بل قصر لأنه مسافر ، وقد مر أن وطن الإقامة يبطل بالسفر فوطن السكني أولى. قوله: (فلا يبطل به وطن (الإقامة والأصلي أولى قوله : (ولا يبطل السفر) أي حكم السفر من قصر الصلاة وغيره والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم وأستغفر الله العظيم.
The residence (sakan) is a place where he intends to stay less than half a month while he is a traveler, so it does not nullify the domicile of residence (watan al-iqamah) nor does it nullify travel (safar). His statement: 'while he is a traveler' is not a condition. Al-Zaylai said: The majority hold that the residence of sakan benefits, and that benefit is conceived in one who goes out to a village for a need without intending travel and intends to stay there less than half a month, he completes (prayer). If he goes out from it not for travel, then resolves to travel before entering his city and before he has stayed less than fifteen days in another place, he shortens (prayer). If he passes by that village, he completes (prayer) because there has not occurred from him what would nullify it from what is above it or similar. End with some change. And his saying 'if he goes out from it not for travel' is a condition because if he goes out from it for travel, it is nullified by agreement. And his saying 'then resolves to travel before entering his city and before he has stayed' etc. is a condition because if he entered his city, it would be nullified by what is above it, i.e., the original homeland (watan asli), and if he stayed in a place less than the period of residence, it would be nullified by its like. In al-Nahr: What is in al-Zaylai is rejected; rather he shortens because he is a traveler, and it has passed that watan al-iqamah is nullified by travel, so watan al-sakan is even more appropriate. His statement: 'So it does not nullify watan al-iqamah', and the original is even more appropriate. His statement: 'nor does it nullify travel' means the rule of travel such as shortening prayer and other things. And Allah most high knows best, and I seek forgiveness from Allah the Magnificent.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the ruling of watan al-sakan (domicile of residence). It first states the basic ruling: a place where a traveler intends to stay less than half a month does not become his watan al-iqamah (domicile of residence that nullifies travel) and does not nullify the legal status of travel (safar). The commentator then refines the understanding by quoting al-Zaylai, who explains that the benefit of watan al-sakan is conceived in a scenario where a person goes to a village for a need without intending travel and stays less than half a month: he must complete his prayer (i.e., not shorten). However, if he leaves that village not for travel, then later decides to travel before entering his home city and before staying fifteen days in another place, he may shorten. The commentator (al-Tahtawi) then critiques al-Zaylai's view, citing al-Nahr that the correct view is that such a person shortens because he remains a traveler. The passage emphasizes that watan al-sakan is weaker than watan al-iqamah and does not affect travel status. The Hanafi position is that watan al-sakan does not nullify travel, and the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that a traveler entering a place with intention to stay less than 15 days remains a traveler and shortens prayer.

Key Terms
وطن السكنى (watan al-sakan): A place where a traveler intends to stay less than half a month (15 days), which does not become a permanent residence and does not nullify the legal status of travel.
وطن الإقامة (watan al-iqamah): A place where a traveler intends to stay for half a month (15 days) or more, which nullifies the status of travel and necessitates completing prayer.
وطن أصلي (watan asli): A person's original homeland (city of birth or permanent residence), which immediately nullifies travel status upon entry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in this passage, what is the ruling for a traveler who enters a village with the intention of staying 10 days?
A) He must complete prayer because he intends to stay more than 3 days.
B) He may shorten prayer because his stay is less than 15 days.
C) He must complete prayer if the village is close to his hometown.
D) He may shorten only if he does not own property in the village.

Key Takeaway
Watan al-sakan (temporary stay under 15 days) does not nullify travel; the traveler retains the license to shorten prayer unless he establishes watan al-iqamah or enters his watan asli.
